Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Transmission of the Right to Qisas
Single maxim stating intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn on how the right to qisas is transmitted to heirs, and explain the practical difference between them.

Core Idea
The right to qisas passes by khilafah (Abu Hanifa) or wirathah (Sahibayn)—determining who can pardon or demand retribution.

This lesson examines a foundational intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal mechanism that transfers the right to seek retribution for intentional homicide from the victim to their relatives. The answer determines whether each heir can independently pardon or demand qisas, or whether a single representative acts for all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State that Abu Hanifa holds the path is khilafah (representation), while the two Sahibayn hold it is wirathah (inheritance).
Memorize the key terms: khilafah and wirathah.
2
Understand the basis of each position
Abu Hanifa views qisas as a personal bodily right that cannot be divided; it transfers by a single representative. The Sahibayn analogize qisas to property rights, inheritable like any other wealth.
Both views are internally consistent within Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If multiple heirs exist, under the Sahibayn each can forgive or claim qisas independently; under Abu Hanifa the designated representative (usually the nearest male) acts for all, so individual pardons may not automatically bind the group.
This difference can affect settlement and diyah negotiations.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the Sahibayn: the right to qisas is inherited.
This is the majority view within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقِصَاصُ طَرِيقُهُ الْخِلَافَةُ عِنْدَهُ وَعِنْدَهُمَا طَرِيقُهُ الْوَرَاثَةُ
Qisas: its path is khilāfah (appointment/caliphate) according to him (Abu Hanifa), and according to them (the two Sahibayn) its path is inheritance.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
Intra-Hanafi dispute: This maxim discusses the legal mechanism by which the right to demand qisās is transmitted to the heirs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the right to qisās is not inherited (like property) but rather the heirs act as successors (khilāfah) to the victim in pursuing retribution. The right is a personal right that passes to the heirs as representatives, not as inheritors. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), the right to qisās is inherited like other properties; it passes to the heirs by inheritance (wirāthah). The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa's view, if all heirs waive qisās, it may lapse; under the Sahibayn's view, each heir inherits a share and can pursue or pardon independently. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is the view of the Sahibayn (inheritance).

Key Terms
خلافة (khilāfah): Succession by appointment or representation, not by inheritance.
وراثة (wirāthah): Inheritance: transmission of rights and obligations from the deceased to heirs by shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, how is the right to qisas transmitted?
A) By khilafah (representation)
B) By wirathah (inheritance)
C) By both equally
D) By judicial appointment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon view is that the right to qisas is inherited by all heirs equally, not held by a single representative.

The Maxim of Evidentiary Equivalence
Legal maxim on evidentiary equivalence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-thābit bi al-shahādah ka al-thābit mu'āyanatan' and explain its legal implications in Hanafi courtroom procedure.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, facts proven by credible testimony carry the same legal certainty as direct observation.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi evidence law: what is established by acceptable witness testimony is treated as if the judge himself observed it. This maxim underpins the reliability of judicial verdicts based on shahādah and ensures that due process is maintained. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping how courts weigh evidence.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Arabic maxim
Commit the phrase to memory. Understanding its exact wording is the first step.
The maxim is concise and often quoted.
2
Define each key term
Break down thābit, shahādah, and mu'āyanah. Understand their legal meanings.
Refer to the key terms section.
3
Identify the legal equivalence
Recognize that the maxim states a legal equivalence, not a metaphysical certainty. Testimony is treated as equivalent to observation after meeting conditions.
This is a procedural rule.
4
Consider the conditions
The equivalence holds only when shahādah meets the Hanafi standards: number of witnesses, credibility, etc.
If conditions are not met, the testimony may be rejected.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a simple court case, determine the ruling based on the maxim.
Practice task will cover this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّابِتُ بِالشَّهَادَةِ كَالثَّابِتِ مُعَايَنَةً
That which is established by testimony is like that which is established by direct observation.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
Ruling statement: This is a general legal principle (qā'idah fiqhiyyah) applicable in courts and evidence law. It means that facts proven by admissible witness testimony (shahādah) carry the same legal weight as facts known through direct observation (mu'āyanah). Therefore, a verdict based on the testimony of credible witnesses is considered as certain as if the judge had witnessed the event himself. This is particularly important in criminal and property cases. However, this applies when the testimony meets the strict conditions (e.g., number of witnesses, credibility) required by Islamic law.

Key Terms
ثابت (thābit): Established or proven in court, whether by evidence or observation.
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony; the statement of a witness in court.
معاينة (mu'āyanah): Direct observation or eyewitnessing by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In court, what is the legal status of a fact proven by two credible witnesses according to the Hanafi maxim?
A) Less reliable than seeing it with one's own eyes
B) As reliable as direct observation
C) Only acceptable for property cases
D) Not sufficient for criminal cases

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-thābit bi al-shahādah ka al-thābit mu'āyanatan' establishes that properly authenticated witness testimony carries the same legal force as direct observation by the judge.

The Maxim: Al-Muṭlaq Yughāyir al-Muqayyad
Legal maxim on interpretation of absolute and qualified terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muṭlaq and muqayyad and state the Hanafi rule that they are distinct unless the ruling and cause are identical.

Core Idea
Muṭlaq and muqayyad are legally distinct; harmony requires identical ruling and cause.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim of Hanafi legal interpretation: 'The unqualified differs from the qualified.' Understanding this principle is critical for correctly deriving rulings from the Qur'an and Sunnah. For instance, when one verse commands freeing a slave and another specifies a believing slave, Hanafi methodology treats them as different unless certain conditions are met.

Guided Path
1
Define muṭlaq and muqayyad
Learn the basic definitions: muṭlaq is unrestricted, muqayyad is restricted by a condition or attribute.
Muṭlaq applies to all individuals of a genus; muqayyad restricts to those meeting the qualification.
2
State the maxim
Recite the Arabic maxim and understand its literal meaning: 'The unqualified differs from the qualified.'
This is a principle of interpretation, not a rule of contradiction.
3
Apply the maxim to a textual example
Consider the Qur'anic verses: 'Free a slave' (muṭlaq) and 'Free a believing slave' (muqayyad). According to Hanafi usul, these are treated as distinct rulings unless the underlying reason (sabab) and the ruling (ḥukm) are the same.
If the cause and ruling differ, the muqayyad does not restrict the muṭlaq; each applies in its own context.
4
Note the Hanafi condition for harmonization
Remember that Hanafis only allow the muqayyad to restrict the muṭlaq when the ruling and the cause (sabab) are identical. Otherwise, they remain independent.
This is a point of difference with the Shāfiʿī school, which restricts the muṭlaq unconditionally if both appear in the same context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمُطْلَقَ يُغَايِرُ الْمُقَيَّدَ
The unqualified differs from the qualified.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
Ruling statement: This is a principle of Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh (legal theory) that governs interpretation of texts. An absolute (muṭlaq) expression applies to all instances without restriction, while a qualified (muqayyad) expression is restricted by a condition or attribute. They are legally distinct unless there is an indication (qarīnah) that they are equivalent. For example, the Qur'an commands to free a slave (muṭlaq) in expiation for some sins, but in other places specifies 'a believing slave' (muqayyad). According to Hanafi principles, the muqayyad does not restrict the muṭlaq unless the ruling and reason are the same. This maxim reminds that one cannot automatically apply the same ruling to both without analysis.

Key Terms
مطلق (muṭlaq): An expression that is unrestricted and applies to all individuals of a genus.
مقيد (muqayyad): An expression that is restricted by a qualification, condition, or attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, if the Qur'an says 'free a slave' in one verse and 'free a believing slave' in another, which statements apply?
A) The muṭlaq always applies
B) The muqayyad always restricts the muṭlaq
C) They are considered different unless the ruling and cause are the same
D) They are equivalent and the muqayyad is ignored

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, muṭlaq and muqayyad are treated as distinct unless the same ruling and cause are present.

Four Key Maxims from the Chapter on Injuries
List of four legal maxims from the chapter on injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four maxims and explain the legal principle behind each one.

Core Idea
The wound of a dumb animal is without liability; ta'jil in specific goods is void; sirayah applies only to legal descriptions; ghasb cuts off sirayah.

This passage from the Hidayah presents four concise legal maxims from the chapter on injuries. These maxims govern liability for animal-caused injuries, the validity of time stipulations in contracts for specific goods, the scope of legal extension (sirayah), and the effect of usurpation (ghasb) on liability. Memorizing these will help you quickly recall key principles in tort and contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of each maxim
Read each maxim and determine whether it deals with liability, contracts, legal extension, or usurpation.
For example, the first maxim is about liability for animal injuries.
2
Understand the legal rationale
Ask why each maxim holds. For instance, why is ta'jil in 'ayan invalid? Because it introduces uncertainty (gharar).
The rationale is often based on avoiding harm or uncertainty.
3
Connect to broader Hanafi principles
Consider how these maxims relate to other Hanafi rules, e.g., sirayah only applies to legal descriptions because real descriptions are not transferable.
This helps build a network of principles.
4
Apply each maxim to a simple scenario
Test your understanding by applying each maxim to a hypothetical case from daily life.
For example, a stray dog bites someone; under maxim 63, the owner is not liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية الملوك والجناية عليه
(63) جُرْحُ الْعَجُمَاءِ جُبَارٌ
(24) اَلتَّأْجِيلُ فِي الْأَعْيَانِ بَاطِلٌ
(25) اَلسِّرَايَةُ فِي الْأَوْصَافِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ دُونَ الْأَوْصَافِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ
(26) الْغَصْبُ قَاطِع لِلسَّرَايَةِ
Chapter on injuries caused by animals and injury against them. (63) The wound caused by a dumb animal is without liability (hubar). (24) Stipulating a term in matters of [selling] specific goods is invalid. (25) Extension (sirayah) applies to legal descriptions but not to real descriptions. (26) Usurpation (ghasb) cuts off extension (sirayah).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents a set of legal maxims from the chapter on injuries (jinaayaat). The first maxim states that if an animal causes harm, the owner is not liable (jubaar). The second maxim (24) deals with contracts for specific goods (a'yaan): any stipulation of a future term (ta'jeel) is invalid because it introduces uncertainty. The third maxim (25) distinguishes between legal descriptions (e.g., being a slave, being owned) and real descriptions (e.g., being healthy): only legal descriptions can 'spread' (sirayah) effects to other attributes. The fourth maxim (26) says that if property has been wrongfully taken (ghasb), subsequent changes (like damage) do not extend liability beyond the act of usurpation itself.

Key Terms
جَرْحُ الْعَجُمَاءِ جُبَارٌ (jarḥ al-ʿajmā’ jibār): The wound caused by a dumb animal is without liability; the owner does not have to pay compensation.
تَأْجِيل (taʾjīl): Stipulating a future time for delivery or payment in a contract for specific goods.
سِرَايَة (sirayah): Legal extension or spreading of an attribute or ruling from one element to another.
غَصْب (ghasb): Wrongful taking or usurpation of another's property without consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (24), why is ta'jil (stipulation of a term) in contracts for specific goods invalid?
A) Because it leads to uncertainty (gharar)
B) Because the goods may perish before delivery
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because it is only allowed in debts

Key Takeaway
Four maxims govern liability, contract validity, and the spread of legal effects in Hanafi law.

Liability of a Person Under Legal Interdiction (Mahjur)
Legal maxim on liability of the mahjur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a mahjur is civilly liable for his harmful acts and identify the rationale based on protecting victims' rights.

Core Idea
A minor or insane person is liable for his torts even though his contracts are void.

This lesson examines a foundational maxim in Hanafi fiqh regarding the liability of those under legal interdiction (mahjur). In Hanafi law, a mahjur (such as a minor or an insane person) is not legally competent to engage in valid transactions, but he is nonetheless held responsible for the harm he causes to others. This principle ensures that victims are compensated regardless of the perpetrator's legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the category of mahjur
Identify who qualifies as a mahjur: minor, insane person, slave without permission.
Mahjur is defined by lack of legal capacity for transactions.
2
State the ruling of the maxim
The mahjur is liable for his actions (af'al).
Liability is for torts, not contracts.
3
Apply to a scenario
Example: A minor breaks a window; he must compensate.
No consideration of intent.
4
Distinguish from transactions
Mahjur's transactions are void, but torts incur liability.
Rationale: protecting huquq al-ibad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
(67) الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤَاخَذْ بِأَفْعَالِهِ
(28) شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزَّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدِّيًا
(29) صِحَّةُ الْقَصْدِ لَا مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي حُقُوقِ الْعِبَادِ
Chapter on usurpation of the slave, the mudabbar, and the minor, and injury in that regard. (67) The person under interdiction (mahjur) is held accountable for his actions. (28) The condition of the effective cause ('illa) is given the status of the 'illa itself when it involves transgression (ta'addi). (29) The soundness of intention is not considered in rights of people (huquq al-'ibad).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage contains three maxims. The first (67) states that even a minor or insane person (mahjur 'alayh) can be held liable for his torts—this is a Hanafi position to protect victims. The second maxim (28) is a principle of legal reasoning: when a condition (shart) is part of a transgression, it is treated like the actual cause for liability. For example, the condition of a loan being interest-bearing is treated like the interest itself. The third maxim (29) states that in matters of civil liability (huquq al-'ibad), the actor's intention does not matter—only the act and its consequences count. So, even if one kills accidentally, full compensation is due.

Key Terms
مَحْجُور (maḥjūr): A person under legal interdiction, such as a minor, insane person, or a slave without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about a mahjur according to this maxim?
A) He is not liable for torts because of lack of capacity.
B) He is liable for his torts.
C) He is only liable if he is discerning.
D) His guardian is always liable.

Key Takeaway
A mahjur (minor, insane, etc.) is civilly liable for his torts, ensuring victims' rights are protected.

Condition of the Cause Given the Status of the Cause in Transgression
Legal maxim on condition of the cause in transgression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi usul principle that a condition of the cause (shart al-illa) is treated like the cause when it involves transgression (ta'addi), and apply it to cases of liability.

Core Idea
If a condition is part of a wrongful act, it is legally equivalent to the cause for liability.

This lesson introduces a subtle principle of legal reasoning used in Hanafi fiqh, particularly in the context of torts and unlawful acts. The principle states that when a condition (shart) of an effective cause ('illa) is itself a transgression (ta'addi), that condition is treated as the cause itself for the purpose of liability. This allows the jurist to extend rulings from the direct cause to its preparatory conditions, ensuring that justice is served.

Guided Path
1
Define the terms: shart, 'illa, ta'addi
Shart is a condition; 'illa is the effective cause; ta'addi means transgression or wrongful act.
The condition is something without which the cause would not operate.
2
Understand the principle
If the condition itself is a transgression, it is given the same legal status as the cause.
Example: lending money at interest – the condition of interest is treated like the interest itself.
3
Apply to a tort scenario
If someone sets up a condition that leads to harm, he is liable even if the direct cause is someone else's act? Actually, need a clear example.
The commentary mentions loan with interest.
4
Recognize the scope
This principle applies only when ta'addi is present; otherwise, condition and cause remain distinct.
It is an exception to the general rule that condition is not part of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
(67) الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤَاخَذْ بِأَفْعَالِهِ
(28) شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزَّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدِّيًا
(29) صِحَّةُ الْقَصْدِ لَا مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي حُقُوقِ الْعِبَادِ
Chapter on usurpation of the slave, the mudabbar, and the minor, and injury in that regard. (67) The person under interdiction (mahjur) is held accountable for his actions. (28) The condition of the effective cause ('illa) is given the status of the 'illa itself when it involves transgression (ta'addi). (29) The soundness of intention is not considered in rights of people (huquq al-'ibad).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage contains three maxims. The first (67) states that even a minor or insane person (mahjur 'alayh) can be held liable for his torts—this is a Hanafi position to protect victims. The second maxim (28) is a principle of legal reasoning: when a condition (shart) is part of a transgression, it is treated like the actual cause for liability. For example, the condition of a loan being interest-bearing is treated like the interest itself. The third maxim (29) states that in matters of civil liability (huquq al-'ibad), the actor's intention does not matter—only the act and its consequences count. So, even if one kills accidentally, full compensation is due.

Key Terms
شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ (sharṭ al-ʿilla): The condition that is part of the effective cause in legal reasoning; it is given the same ruling as the cause when transgression is involved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key requirement for maxim (28) to apply?
A) The condition must be a necessary part of the cause.
B) The condition must involve transgression (ta'addi).
C) The condition must be explicitly stated.
D) The cause must be a prohibited act.

Key Takeaway
A condition that is itself a transgression is treated as the cause for liability, preventing evasion of prohibitions.

6 min
Intention Irrelevant in Rights of People (Huquq al-‘Ibad)
Legal maxim on intention in civil rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that intention is not considered in civil liability matters (huquq al-ibad) and apply it to determine liability in accidental harm.

Core Idea
In civil rights, liability is strict: the act alone determines compensation, not the actor's intention.

This lesson presents a fundamental principle of Hanafi tort law: when it comes to rights of people (huquq al-ibad), the intention or soundness of purpose (sihhat al-qasd) is irrelevant. What matters is the act itself and its consequences. This means that even if someone causes harm accidentally or without any bad intent, full compensation is due. The same principle underpins the first maxim on liability of the mahjur.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy: huquq Allah vs huquq al-ibad
In acts of worship (huquq Allah), intention is essential. In civil matters (huquq al-ibad), intention is irrelevant.
This is a central distinction in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the maxim
Sihhat al-qasd (soundness of intention) is not considered in huquq al-ibad.
Literal: 'No consideration is given to the soundness of intention.'
3
Apply to a scenario
Example: A person accidentally kills someone while driving. He must pay diyah (blood money) regardless of intent.
Intent matters only for sin or punishment, not liability.
4
Recognize exceptions
Are there any exceptions? Possibly in cases where intent determines the nature of the act (e.g., murder vs. manslaughter). But for liability, compensation is always due.
The maxim is general; some later jurists discuss nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
(67) الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤَاخَذْ بِأَفْعَالِهِ
(28) شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزَّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدِّيًا
(29) صِحَّةُ الْقَصْدِ لَا مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي حُقُوقِ الْعِبَادِ

(28) The condition of the effective cause ('illa) is given the status of the 'illa itself when it involves transgression (ta'addi). (29) The soundness of intention is not considered in rights of people (huquq al-'ibad).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage contains three maxims. The first (67) states that even a minor or insane person (mahjur 'alayh) can be held liable for his torts—this is a Hanafi position to protect victims. The second maxim (28) is a principle of legal reasoning: when a condition (shart) is part of a transgression, it is treated like the actual cause for liability. Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (29), which of the following is true regarding liability for harm to another's property?
A) If the harm was accidental, no compensation is due.
B) If the person had good intentions, liability is reduced.
C) Liability is based solely on the act and its consequences.
D) Intention is considered if the harm was unintentional.

Key Takeaway
In civil matters (huquq al-ibad), compensation is due for harm caused, irrespective of the actor's intention.

Legal Maxims on Qasama and Witness Testimony
List of legal maxims under the chapter on qasama

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five legal maxims from the chapter on qasama and explain their practical implications.

Core Idea
The oath is a valid statement; qasama follows probability; witness disqualification is by consensus.

This lesson presents a series of concise legal maxims from the Hanafi chapter on qasama. These maxims clarify when an oath can establish a right, when qasama becomes obligatory, and when a witness is disqualified after being a litigant. Understanding these principles is key to applying Hanafi law in homicide cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first maxim (70)
Understand that 'the oath is a valid statement' means an oath can serve as legal proof (qawl sahih) in court.
This establishes the evidentiary value of oaths.
2
Identify the second maxim (71)
Qasama is only applied when there is probability (ihtimal) that the accused committed the homicide.
Without probability, qasama is not required.
3
Identify the third maxim (72)
If a body is found in a place where, were other bodies found, qasama would apply, then qasama is not obligatory for that single body unless the condition would not hold.
This is a conditional rule about multiple bodies.
4
Identify the fourth maxim (73)
A person who was a claimant in a case and then ceases to be one cannot testify in that case by consensus.
Prevents conflict of interest.
5
Identify the fifth maxim (74)
A person who had the potential to be a claimant but that potential ceases can testify; his testimony is accepted by consensus.
Contrasts with the previous maxim: only actual claim disqualifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسامة
(70) الْيَمِينُ قَولٌ صَحِيحٌ
(1) اَلْقَسَامَةُ تَتْبَعُ احْتِمَالَ الْقَتْلِ
(72) الْمَوْجُودُ الأَوَّلُ إِنْ كَانَ بِحَالٍ لَوْ وُجِدَ الْبَاقِي تَجْرِى فِيهِ الْقَسَامَةُ لَا تَجِبُ فِيهِ وَإِلَّا فَتَجِبُ فِيهِ
(73) مَنِ انْتَصَبَ خَصُمًا فِى حَادِثَةٍ ثُمَّ خَرَجَ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ خَصْمًا لَمْ تُقْبَلُ شَهَادَتُهُ فِي تِلْكَ الْحَادِثَةِ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ
(74) إِذَا كَانَتْ لِرَجُلٍ عَرْضِيَّةُ أَنْ يَصِيرَ خَصْمًا ثُمَّ بَطَلَتْ تِلْكَ الْعَرْضِيَّةُ فَشَهِدَ قُبِلَتْ شَهَادَتُهُ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ
Chapter on qasama. (70) The oath is a valid statement. (1) Qasama follows the probability of homicide. (72) The first existing [body] if in a condition that were the rest found qasama would apply to it, then qasama is not obligatory; otherwise it is obligatory. (73) Whoever becomes a claimant in a case, then ceases to be a claimant, his testimony in that case is not accepted by consensus. (74) If a man had the potential to become a claimant, but that potential ceases, and then he testifies, his testimony is accepted by consensus.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage covers important rules about qasama (a collective oath in homicide cases when there is strong suspicion but no eyewitness). The first maxim (70) asserts that an oath is a valid statement (qawl sahih) in legal proceedings, meaning it can establish rights. The second maxim (71) explains that qasama is based on the probability (ihtimal) that the accused committed the murder – it is not required if there is no such probability. The third maxim (72) is more technical: if a body is found in a place such that if other bodies were also found, qasama would apply, then qasama is not required for that single body unless the condition would not hold. The final two maxims (73-74) deal with the disqualification of a witness who was previously a litigant in the same case – by consensus, such a witness is not accepted.

Key Terms
قَسَامَة (qasāma): A collective oath taken by the heirs of a homicide victim when there is strong suspicion but no direct evidence; it obligates the accused to pay blood money.
يَمِين (yamīn): An oath taken in court, considered a valid statement that can prove or disprove a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (73), why is a person who was a claimant in a case then ceases to be one prevented from testifying in that same case?
A) Because his testimony would be biased
B) By consensus, to avoid conflict of interest
C) Because he might lie
D) Because he is no longer a party

Key Takeaway
The five maxims define the legal framework for qasama and witness testimony in Hanafi law.

Eight Legal Maxims from al-Hidayah: Wills and Hermaphrodites
Lists eight legal maxims from Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight legal maxims regarding wills and hermaphrodites and explain their practical application in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Legal maxims distill core principles: wills follow intent, sin wills are void, hermaphrodites require caution.

This lesson presents eight foundational maxims from al-Hidayah that guide jurists in matters of wills (waṣāyā) and rulings on hermaphrodites (khunthā). These maxims are not independent rulings but hermeneutical principles that help derive and apply the law consistently. Memorizing them will sharpen your legal intuition and provide a framework for analyzing complex cases in inheritance and gender ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domain
Separate the maxims into two groups: wills (87-91) and hermaphrodites (92-94). Note the chapter headings in the source.
Maxims 87-90 fall under the Chapter of Bequest of Residence and Service; 91 under Chapter of Bequest of a Dhimmī; 92-94 under Book of the Hermaphrodite.
2
Understand key Arabic terms
For each maxim, identify the critical term (e.g., al-muṭlaq, al-ḥaqīqa, al-waṣiyya, al-āmm, al-khunthā). Use the key terms list for definitions.
These terms are the building blocks of the maxims.
3
State the maxim in English
Translate each maxim from Arabic to English, ensuring you capture the legal nuance. Compare with the provided translation.
The translation should be precise; avoid paraphrasing.
4
Explain the legal consequence
State what the maxim mandates or prohibits. For example, maxim (87) means that an unqualified term is interpreted literally unless context specifies otherwise.
Think about how this would affect a judge's decision.
5
Provide a simple example
Create a concrete scenario for each maxim to illustrate its application. For instance, a will saying 'I give my house to Zaid' – the word 'house' is unrestricted and means the literal building (maxim 87).
Use everyday situations involving bequests or gender ambiguity.

(۸۷) الْمُطْلَقُ يَنْصَرِفُ إِلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ
باب الوصية بالسكنى والخدمة
(۸۸) الْوَصِيَّةُ تَنْفُذُ عَلَى مَا يُعْرَفُ مِنْ مَقْصُودِ الْمُوصِى
(۸۹) الْمُوْصَى لَهُ بِالظُّرْفِ لَا يُشَارِكُ الْمُوْصَى لَهُ بِالْمَظْرُوفِ
(۹۰) الْعَامُ الَّذِى مُوجِبُهُ تُبُوتُ الْحُكْمِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْإِحَاطَةِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْخَاصٌ

فصل في أحكام الخنثى
(۹۲) الأصْلُ فِى الحُلّى المُشكل أن يُوعَدُ فِيهِ بِالأحْوَطِ وَالْأَوْثَقِ فِي أُمُورِ الدِّينِ، وَأَنْ لَا يَحْكُمَ بِثْبُوتِ حُكْمِ وَقَعَ الشَّكُ فِي ثُبُوتِهِ

(۹۴) الْكِتَابَةُ مِمَّنْ نَأَى بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْخِطَابِ مِمَّنْ دَنَا
Principles of Hidayah, Fourth Volume
(87) The unrestricted (term) is directed to the literal meaning.
Chapter of bequest of residence and service
(88) The will is executed according to what is known of the testator's intent.
(89) The legatee of the container does not share with the legatee of the contents.
(90) The general (term) whose implication necessitates establishment of a ruling in a comprehensive manner is like the specific.
Chapter of bequest of a dhimmī
(91) A will to commit a sin is void.
Book of the Hermaphrodite
Section on rulings on the hermaphrodite
(92) The principle regarding ambiguous matters is that one should act with the most precautionary and trustworthy in religious matters, and that one should not rule on the establishment of a ruling whose establishment is doubted.
(93) Gesture is only considered when it becomes customary and known.
(94) Writing from one who is far is like speech from one who is near.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents eight foundational legal maxims from the Hidayah, primarily concerning wills (waṣāyā) and the rulings on hermaphrodites (khunthā). The first maxim (87) states that when a term is used without qualification, it is understood in its literal, real sense—a key principle of linguistic interpretation in Hanafi uṣūl. The following maxims (88-91) address wills: a will is executed based on the testator's known intent; a legatee of a container (like a house) does not share with a legatee of its contents; a general term comprehensive in effect is treated like a specific one; and a will to commit a sin is void. The final three maxims (92-94) deal with hermaphrodites and ambiguous matters: one must act cautiously in religious matters when doubt exists; a gesture is valid only if it is customary and clearly understood; and writing from an absent person is equivalent to speech from someone present. These maxims guide judges and jurists in applying the law consistently.

Key Terms
الْمُطْلَق (al-muṭlaq): An unqualified or unrestricted term that carries its literal meaning without context limiting it.
الْحَقِيقَة (al-ḥaqīqa): The literal, real meaning of a term as opposed to metaphorical.
الْوَصِيَّة (al-waṣiyya): A bequest or will; a disposition of property after death.
الْخَنثَى (al-khunthā): Hermaphrodite; a person with ambiguous genitalia, whose sex is unclear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (91), what is the ruling on a will made to commit a sin?
A) It is valid but makruh.
B) It is void (bāṭil).
C) It is suspended until repentance.
D) It is valid only if the sin is minor.

Key Takeaway
These eight maxims provide a concise framework for interpreting wills and handling gender ambiguity, emphasizing textual literalism, intent, and precaution.

Mubah Property Belongs to the First Taker
Explaining the principle that ownerless property belongs to the first taker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for ownership of mubah property and apply it to scenarios involving wildlife on private land.

Core Idea
Ownerless property belongs to the first taker, even on another's land.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational principle of Hanafi property law: that which is originally ownerless (mubah) becomes owned by the first person to take possession of it. This includes wild animals, birds, eggs, and similar items. We will see how this rule applies even when such items appear on land owned by someone else, unless that landowner took specific preparatory steps.

Guided Path
1
Define mubah property and its characteristics
Identify what qualifies as mubah: any property that is not owned and permissible to take (e.g., wild birds, fish, deer). Understand that it is not initially owned by anyone.
2
Understand the criterion for ownership: priority of possession (sabq al-yad)
The first person to physically take hold of the mubah property becomes its owner. Mere sight or intention is not enough; actual physical possession (al-yad) is required.
3
Examine the exception: landowner does not automatically own things on his land
If a bird hatches chicks, lays eggs, or a deer makes a den on someone's land, the landowner does not own them unless he specifically prepared the land for that purpose (e.g., set up a nest or feeder). Without preparation, the first taker owns the item.
This exception prevents the landowner from claiming ownership of wild resources he did not cultivate.
4
Apply the principle to the examples from Al-Hidayah
Work through the given examples: bird hatching, laying eggs, deer making a den. In each, the landowner did not prepare, so the first taker owns the item. Note that this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is universally agreed upon.
Consistency across examples reinforces the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ ثالث
۷۴
(۳۹) اَلْمُبَاحُ لِمَنْ سَبَقَتْ يَدُهُ إِلَيْهِ.
ترجمہ: مباح چیز اُس کی ہوگی جس نے پہلے حاصل کی ہو۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو چیزیں مباح الاصل ہوں یعنی تمام لوگوں کے لئے وہ مباح ہوں تو ہ چیز آخند کی ہوگی ،اگر چہ وہ چیز کسی کی مملوکہ زمین سے حاصل کی ہو۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ ایک شخص نے اپنی زمین میں کوئی کاشت کاری کی اور پھر کسی پرندے نے اس زمین میں بچے جن دیئے یا انڈے دے دیئے ، یا کسی ہرن نے اس میں اپنا گھر بنالیا اور صاحب ارض نے ان چیزوں میں سے کسی بھی چیز کے لیے زمین کو تیار نہیں کیا تھا، تو اس صورت میں خاص صاحب ارض اس کا مالک نہیں ہوگا، بلکہ جو شخص بھی پہلے انہیں حاصل کرے گا وہی اس کا مالک ہوگا، اس لیے کہ ان میں سے ہر ایک چیز مباح ہے اور مباح چیز کے متعلق اصول یہ ہے کہ جو پہلے پکڑتا اور لیتا ہے وہی اس کا مالک ہوتا ہے۔
الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب السلم مسائل منثورة، ج ۳ ص . وَإِنْ أَفَرَخَ طَيْرٌ ، أَوْ بَاضَ ، أَوْ تَكَنَّسَ ظَبُيٍّ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ، فَهُوَ لِمَنْ أَخَذَهُ) لأَنَّهُ مُبَاحٌ سَبَقَتْ يَدُهُ إِلَيْهِ فَكَانَ، أَوْلَى بِهِ. تبيين الحقائق: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، باب المتفرقات، ج ۴ ص ۱۳۰) قَالَ (وَإِذَا أَفْرَخَ طَيْرٌ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ إِلَحُ) إِذَا أَفْرَخَ طَيْرٌ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ وَلَمْ يُعِدَّهَا لِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَمْلِكُهُ فَهُوَ لِمَنْ أَخَذَهُ ، وَكَذَا إِذَا بَاضَ فِيهَا أَوْ تَكَنَّسَ فِيهَا ظَبُى.
Usool E Hidayah Volume 3 74 (39) Mubah property belongs to the one whose hand precedes it. Translation: The mubah thing will belong to the one who first obtains it. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that things that are originally mubah, i.e., permissible for all people, then that thing will belong to the obtainer, even if that thing is obtained from someone's owned land. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, explains that if a person cultivated his land, and then a bird hatched chicks or laid eggs in that land, or a deer made its home in it, and the landowner did not prepare the land for any of these things, then in this case the specific landowner will not be the owner, but rather whoever first obtains them will be the owner, because each of these things is mubah, and the principle regarding mubah things is that whoever first catches and takes it, he is the owner. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, Masa'il Manthura, vol. 3, p. : 'And if a bird hatches, or lays eggs, or a deer makes a den in a man's land, then it belongs to the one who takes it, because it is mubah, his hand preceded it, so he is more entitled to it.' (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, Bab al-Mutafarriqat, vol. 4, p. 130) He said: 'And if a bird hatches in a man's land...' - if a bird hatches in a man's land and he did not prepare it for that, he does not own it, so it belongs to the one who takes it. Similarly, if it lays eggs in it or a deer makes a den in it.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling from the Hanafi school, defining the ownership of mubah (ownerless) property. It states that any item that is originally permissible and not owned by anyone belongs to the first person to take possession of it, even if it appears on someone's private land. The author uses examples: a bird hatching chicks, laying eggs, or a deer making a den on someone's land. If the landowner did not specifically prepare the land for these things (e.g., by setting up a nest or feeding the animals), he does not automatically own them. Instead, whoever first captures them becomes the owner. The proof is the principle itself (mubah belongs to the first taker), which is based on general permissibility and the maxim that priority gives entitlement. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is universally agreed upon among schools (muttafaq alayh) in principle, though details may vary.

Key Terms
مباح (mubah): Property that is ownerless and permissible for anyone to take, such as wild birds, eggs, or deer.
سبق اليد (sabq al-yad): Priority of possession; the first person to physically take the property becomes the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A bird lays eggs in a man's field. The man did not prepare the field for birds. Who owns the eggs according to the Hanafi school?
A) The landowner, because it is on his land.
B) Whoever first picks up the eggs.
C) The government, because it is public property.
D) No one, because it is not owned.

Key Takeaway
Mubah property belongs to the first taker; land ownership alone does not confer ownership of wild resources appearing on it.

Mubah Property Belongs to the First Taker
Ruling that birds' nests in land belong to the first taker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of mubah items found on another's land and apply the principle that first possession confers ownership.

Core Idea
Ownerless things belong to the first person to take them, even on someone else's land.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a bird lays eggs or a gazelle makes a lair on your property. Do you automatically own them? The Hanafi school says no—unless you prepared for them. Instead, ownership goes to the first person who physically takes possession. This ruling is based on the general principle that mubah (ownerless) property becomes owned by the first taker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize the specific case: a bird hatches chicks, lays eggs, or a gazelle makes a lair on a man's land. The ruling: it belongs to whoever takes it (first taker).
The landowner does not own it automatically.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the general principle that mubah belongs to the first taker. This is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever precedes to something mubah, it is his.'
The text explicitly says 'because it is mubah and his hand preceded to it'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The rationale: Since the item is mubah (ownerless), no one has prior ownership. The first person to physically possess it acquires ownership through ihraz (taking possession).
This is a talil (ratio) based on the nature of mubah.
4
Note the condition
A key condition: the landowner did not prepare the land for the birds/gazelle (e.g., did not build a nest). If he had, the chicks/eggs would belong to him.
This condition is implied by the phrase 'and he did not prepare the land for that'.
5
Consider the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that the landowner's permission is required or that he has a stronger claim. The Hanafi position is that mere land ownership does not confer ownership of wild produce.
The practical difference: under Shafi'i, the landowner might be entitled to the eggs; under Hanafi, the first taker owns them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أَفْرَخَ طَيْرٌ ، أَوْ بَاضَ ، أَوْ تَكَنَّسَ ظَبْيٍ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ، فَهُوَ لِمَنْ أَخَذَهُ) لِأَنَّهُ مُبَاحٌ سَبَقَتْ يَدُهُ إِلَيْهِ فَكَانَ، أَوْلَى بِهِ. قَالَ (وَإِذَا أَفْرَخَ طَيْرٌ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ إلخ) إِذَا أَفْرَخَ طَيْرٌ فِي أَرْضِ رَجُلٍ وَلَمْ يُعِدَّهَا لِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَمْلِكْهُ فَهُوَ لِمَنْ أَخَذَهُ ، وَكَذَا إِذَا بَاضَ فِيهَا أَوْ تَكَنَّسَ فِيهَا ظَبْيٌ.
If a bird hatches chicks, or lays eggs, or a gazelle makes a lair in a man's land, then it belongs to whoever takes it, because it is mubah (free) and his hand preceded to it, so he is more entitled to it. He said: (When a bird hatches chicks in a man's land etc.) When a bird hatches chicks in a man's land and he did not prepare the land for that, then he does not own it; it belongs to whoever takes it; similarly if it lays eggs there or a gazelle makes a lair there.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on ownership of mubah (ownerless) things. The specific case: a bird hatches chicks, lays eggs, or a gazelle makes a den on a man's land. The owner of the land does not automatically own these things unless he prepared the land for them (e.g., built a nest). Since they are mubah, they belong to the first person who physically takes possession of them. The proof is the general principle: 'Mubah belongs to whoever first acquires it.' This is based on the hadith 'Whoever precedes to something mubah, it is his.' The Hanafi school applies this consistently: mere land ownership does not confer ownership of wildlife or its produce that is not cultivated by the owner. The text cites Al-Hidayah and Tabyin al-Haqaiq to confirm this ruling.

Key Terms
مباح (mubāḥ): In this passage, mubah means ownerless property that anyone is permitted to take and own by possession.
سبقت يده إليه (sabaqat yaduhu ilayhi): His hand preceded to it, meaning he physically took possession before anyone else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A bird hatches chicks in a tree on a man's land. The man did not prepare the nest. Who owns the chicks?
A) The landowner
B) The first person to catch and take the chicks
C) The bird
D) No one

Key Takeaway
Ownerless items on another's land belong to the first taker, not the landowner, unless the landowner prepared for them.

Shari'a Rulings Follow Linguistic Meanings: The Case of Hawala
Applying the principle that shari'a rulings follow linguistic meanings to hawala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that shari'a rulings follow linguistic meanings, apply it to the contract of hawala, and explain why the original debtor is fully discharged.

Core Idea
Hawala transfers the debt completely because its linguistic meaning is 'transfer'; the original debtor is freed, unlike kafala where liability is added.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi fiqh: that the rulings of shari'a are built upon the plain linguistic meanings of terms. The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to explain the legal effect of hawala (transfer of debt). By understanding that 'hawala' literally means to transfer, we derive that the original debtor (muhil) is completely discharged from the debt, and the creditor can only claim from the transferee (muhtal 'alayh). This contrasts with kafala (suretyship), where the surety's liability is added to the principal's.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
The author begins with the principle: 'Shari'a rulings are in accordance with linguistic meanings.' This means that when a term is used in Islamic law, its legal ruling is derived from its ordinary Arabic meaning, unless there is a specific shari'a definition that overrides it.
2
Identify the term and its linguistic meaning
Identify the term 'hawala' and determine its linguistic meaning. In Arabic, 'hawala' means to transfer or shift something from one place to another. This plain meaning is the starting point for the legal analysis.
3
Apply the meaning to the contract
Apply the linguistic meaning of 'transfer' to the contract of hawala: if the word means 'transfer', then in a hawala contract, the debt must be transferred from the liability of the original debtor (muhil) to the liability of the transferee (muhtal 'alayh). This transfer must be complete.
4
Derive the ruling for the muhil
From the complete transfer, derive the ruling: the muhil is fully discharged from the debt. The creditor can no longer demand payment from the muhil, only from the muhtal 'alayh. If the debt remained on the muhil, it would be one debt owed by two persons, which is impossible (muhal).
5
Distinguish from kafala
Contrast hawala with kafala (suretyship). The linguistic meaning of 'kafala' is to join one liability to another (ضَمُ الذِّمَّةِ إِلَى الذِّمَّةِ). Therefore, in kafala, the surety's liability is added to the principal's; the principal is not discharged. This distinction confirms that the ruling depends on linguistic meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْأحْكَامُ الشَّرْعِيَّةُ عَلَى وِفَاقِ الْمَعَانِي اللُّغَوِيَّةِ. ترجمه احکام شرعیہ کا دارومداراغوی معانی پر ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شریعت کے احکام کا دار ومدار الفاظ کے لغوی معانی پر ہوتا ہے یعنی حکم لغوی معانی پر مرتب ہوتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ انتلا ر ہے ہیں کہ حوالہ میں جب کھیل (یعنی مقروش و مدیون ) جب کسی دوسرے کے حوالہ کر دے یعنی وقتال علیہ ( جو حوالہ کو قبول کرتا ہے) تو محیل بری ہو جائے گا، اب محتال له ( قرض خواہ) محیل سے مطالبہ نہیں کرسکتا بلکہ صرف محتال علیہ سے مطالبہ کرے گا، اس لیے کہ حوالہ کا معنی ہے نقل کرنا ، حوالہ میں جب دین محیل کے ذمہ سے محتال علیہ کے ذمہ کی طرف منتقل ہو گیا تو اب محیل کا ذمہ فارغ ہو گیا ، اس لیے کہ حوالہ کے لغوی معنی ہیں منتقل ہونا اور یہ انتقال تب ہو گا جب دین میل کے ذمے سے مکمل طور پر منتقل ہو جائے ، اگر دین میل کے ذمہ بھی باقی ہو تو ایک دین کا دو زموں میں ہونا لازم آئے گا جو محال ہے، برخلاف کفالہ کے اس کا لغوی معنى ضم الذمة إلى الذمة.
The shari'a rulings are in accordance with linguistic meanings. Translation: The shari'a rulings depend on linguistic meanings. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the rulings of shari'a depend on the linguistic meanings of words, i.e., the ruling is based on the linguistic meaning. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, explains that in hawala (transfer of debt), when the debtor (i.e., the borrower/obligor) transfers his debt to someone else, i.e., to the muhtal 'alayh (the one who accepts the transfer), then the muhil (transferor) is discharged. Now the creditor (muhtal lahu) cannot demand from the muhil, but only from the muhtal 'alayh, because the meaning of hawala is to transfer. In hawala, when the debt is transferred from the liability of the muhil to the liability of the muhtal 'alayh, then the liability of the muhil becomes free, because the linguistic meaning of hawala is to transfer. And this transfer only happens when the debt is completely transferred from the liability of the muhil. If the debt also remained on the muhil's liability, then it would necessitate one debt being in two liabilities, which is impossible. Unlike kafala (suretyship), whose linguistic meaning is to join one liability to another.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This lesson is a definition-ruling that sets forth the principle that shari'a rulings are based on the linguistic meanings of terms, and then applies it to the contract of hawala (transfer of debt). The passage begins with the principle: 'Shari'a rulings conform to linguistic meanings.' The Urdu commentary explains that this means the ruling depends on the plain linguistic meaning of the words. The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to explain the ruling of hawala: when a debtor (muhil) transfers his debt to another person (muhtal 'alayh), the muhil is completely discharged from the debt. The creditor (muhtal lahu) can then only demand payment from the muhtal 'alayh, not from the muhil. The reason is that the linguistic meaning of 'hawala' is transfer. Therefore, in a valid hawala, the debt is transferred fully from one liability to another. If the debt were to remain on the muhil, it would result in the same debt being due from two persons simultaneously, which is impossible (muhal). This distinguishes hawala from kafala (suretyship), where the linguistic meaning is 'joining one liability to another' (dam al-dhimma ila al-dhimma), meaning the surety's liability is added to the principal's, not replacing it.

Key Terms
حَوَالَة (hawala): Transfer of debt; a contract where a debtor transfers his debt from his liability to another person's liability, discharging himself.
مُحِيل (muhil): The transferor; the original debtor who transfers his debt to another.
مُحْتَال عَلَيْهِ (muhtal 'alayh): The transferee; the person to whom the debt is transferred and who becomes liable to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the linguistic meaning of 'hawala', what is the legal effect on the original debtor (muhil) after a valid hawala?
A) He remains liable together with the transferee
B) He is completely discharged
C) He becomes a surety
D) He is liable only if the transferee defaults

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the ruling of hawala directly from its linguistic meaning: transfer means full discharge of the original debtor, because a single debt cannot be owed by two persons simultaneously.

The Maxim: Apparent State is Not Proof for Entitlement
Legal maxim on apparent state and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that the apparent state is not proof for entitlement and apply it to a case of inheritance, correctly placing the burden of proof.

Core Idea
Appearance alone cannot establish a legal right; proof must go beyond the present state.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi legal maxim from the chapter on arbitration. The maxim draws a sharp distinction between what is externally visible and what is legally decisive in establishing a claim. Understanding this principle prevents courts from relying on superficial evidence when a party’s right is at stake.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim precisely
Read the Arabic maxim: الظاهر لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق. Translate it and note its source from al-Hidayah, Kitab Adab al-Qadi.
The maxim is a concise legal principle, not a specific ruling on an act.
2
Identify the two key elements: al-ẓāhir and istīḥqāq
Al-ẓāhir is the apparent external state; istīḥqāq is legal entitlement. The maxim denies that the former suffices to prove the latter.
Ḥujja here means a conclusive proof that establishes a right.
3
Work through the given example
Consider a Christian wife whose Muslim husband dies. She later claims she had converted to Islam before his death. Her current apparent Islam (she now appears Muslim) is not accepted as proof that she was Muslim at the time of death. The maxim applies: her apparent state cannot establish the entitlement to inherit.
The wife must provide additional evidence, such as witnesses to her declaration of faith.
4
Determine the burden of proof
The burden lies with the claimant (the wife) to prove the underlying fact (conversion before death). The apparent state alone shifts no burden to the defendant.
This principle prevents reliance on superficial post hoc appearances.
5
Consider the scope of the maxim
The maxim is not limited to inheritance; it applies to any claim of right based on an external appearance, such as possession, marriage, or religious status.
In Hanafi fiqh, ẓāhir is used defensively (e.g., to avoid punishment) but not offensively to claim a right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظَّاهِرُ لَا يَصْلُحُ حُجَّةً لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ. الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ٣ ص ١٥٥
The apparent [state] is not suitable as a proof for entitlement. (Al-Hidayah, Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Bab al-Tahkim, vol. 3, p. 155)
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is a legal maxim from the chapter on arbitration. The principle states that an apparent external state (ẓāhir) is not sufficient evidence to establish a claim of entitlement (istīḥqāq); it can only be used defensively. For example, if a Muslim man dies and his Christian wife later claims she had converted to Islam before his death, her apparent Islam at the time of claim is not accepted as proof that she was Muslim at the time of death. The burden of proof lies with her to provide evidence of the earlier conversion. This principle is from al-Hidayah in the section on adjudication.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-ẓāhir): The apparent or external state of a person or thing, as observed in the present.
حجة (ḥujja): A proof or evidence that can establish a legal claim.
استحقاق (istīḥqāq): Legal entitlement or right to something, such as inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the Christian wife, why is her apparent Islam not accepted as proof of inheritance?
A) Because she is a woman.
B) Because the principle states that apparent state is not a proof for entitlement.
C) Because she did not produce witnesses.
D) Because her husband was Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The apparent external state of a person cannot serve as proof to establish a legal right; substantive evidence is required.

The Principle of Ittihad al-Majlis in Testimony
States the principle of ilhaq when majlis is united

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of ittihad al-majlis and apply it to determine when a correction to testimony is accepted.

Core Idea
A correction made in the same court session is treated as part of the original testimony.

In Hanafi testimony law, the unity of the judicial session (majlis) is crucial. If a witness corrects or adds to his testimony while still in the same session, that correction is not treated as a separate statement but as part of the original. This principle, based on the idea that the session constitutes a single continuous utterance, ensures consistency and prevents abuse. This lesson unpacks the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Maxim
Read the Arabic maxim: الْمَجْلِسُ إِذَا اتَّحَدَ لَحِقَ الْمُلْحَقُ بِأَصْلِ الشَّهَادَةِ. Break it down: al-majlis = the session, idha ittahada = when united, lahiqa = attaches, al-mulhaq = the correction, bi asl al-shahadah = to the original testimony.
The maxim is a concise statement of the rule.
2
Define Key Terms
Define 'majlis' as the court session, 'mulhaq' as the correction or subsequent statement, and 'asl al-shahadah' as the original testimony. Note that in Hanafi fiqh, the unity of these terms is critical.
These terms are specific to the law of testimony (shahadah).
3
Understand the Condition and Effect
The condition for attachment is that the session must be united (ittihad al-majlis). The effect is that the correction is accepted as part of the original testimony, as if they were a single utterance. The witness must also be just (ʿadl).
If the session is not united, the correction is treated as a new testimony.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a witness who testifies in court, then while still in the same session says 'I erred in part of my testimony; the correct amount is X.' According to the principle, this correction is accepted because the majlis is united. The original testimony is now considered with the correction.
This is the exact example from al-Hidayah.
5
Note the Authority
The principle is attributed to Shams al-Aʾimma al-Sarakhsi and is relied upon in the Hanafi school. It is found in al-Hidayah and other major works.
Understanding the source authority strengthens your fiqh knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول بدایہ ثالث
(۱) الْمَجْلِسُ إِذَا اتَّحَدَ لَحِقَ الْمُلْحَقُ بأصل الشَّهَادَةِ.
ترجمہ : جب مجلس متحد ہو تو ملحق اصل شہادت کے ساتھ لاحق ہو گا۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک مرتبہ گواہی دینے کے بعد یہ کہے کہ مجھے سے میری گواہی میں کچھ وہم اور بھول چوک ہوئی ہے اور صحیح گواہی میری یہ ہے تو بعد والی شہادت ملحق کہلاتی ہے، اب اگر مجلس ایک ہو تو یہ لحق اصل شہادت کے ساتھ مل کر قبول ہوگی۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلار ہے ہیں کہ گواہ نے گواہی دی اور اب تک مجلس برقرار تھی کہ گواہ نے یہ کہا کہ أَوْ هَمْتُ بَعْضَ شَهَادَتِی “ کہ مجھ سے گواہی میں کچھ غلطی ہوئی ہے، تو اب اگر گواہ عادل ہو تو گواہی قبول ہوگی کیونکہ گواہ کا أَوْهَمْتُ بَعْضَ شَهَادَنِی “ کہنا ملحق ہے اور دونوں گواہی کی مجلس بھی متحد ہے اور جب مجلس متحد ہو تو ملحق اصل شہادت کے ساتھ لاحق ہوتی ہے، گویا ملحق اور اصل شہادت کلام واحد ہے، لہذا یہ گواہی جو گواہ بعد میں ادا کرتا ہے یہ بھی اصل شہادت کے ساتھ لاحق ہو کر قبول ہوگی ۔
(مجمع الأنهر : كتاب الشهادة، باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل، ج ۲ ص ۲۰۴) على إقرار المدعى بذلك تقبل أى بالجرح بأنه إقران شهودى فسقة فإنها تقبل (لأن الإقرار مما يدخل تحت الحكم أى ما لم تظهره الفاحشة، وإنما حكوها عن غيرهم، وهو المدعى والحاكى لإظهارها ليس كمظهرها وفي بعض النسخ ولو شهدوا على إقرار المدعى بذلك وتقبل البناية كتاب الشهادة، باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل، ج ۹ ص ۱۶۳)
الهداية كتاب الشهادة، باب من يقبل شهادته ومن لا يقبل، ج ۳ ص ۱۷۳ وَفِيهِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى مَا مَالَ إِلَيْهِ شَمْسُ الْأَئِمَّةِ فَإِنَّهُ الْحَقَ الْمُلْحَقَ بِأَصْلِ الشَّهَادَةِ فَصَارَ كَكَلامِ وَاحِدٍ، وَهَذَا يُوجِبُ الْعَمَلَ بِالشَّهَادَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ فِي الزِّيَادَةِ وَالنُّقْصَان. (العناية كتاب الشهادة باب من تقبل الشهادة ومن لا تقبل، ج ص ۴۳۰)
Principle (1): The session, when united, the attached (correction) attaches to the original testimony. Translation: When the session is united, the attached joins with the original testimony. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that after testifying once, if the witness says, 'I was mistaken in part of my testimony; my correct testimony is this,' then the latter statement is called mulhaq (attached). If the session is one, then this attaches to the original testimony and is accepted. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to show that if a witness testifies, and while the session is ongoing, he says, 'I was mistaken in part of my testimony,' then if the witness is just, his testimony is accepted because his statement is mulhaq and the sessions of both testimonies are united. When the session is united, the mulhaq attaches to the original testimony, making them like a single statement. Hence, the testimony given later attaches to the original and is accepted. (References: Majma' al-Anhur, al-Hidayah, al-'Inayah).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle in Hanafi testimony law: the unity of the session (ittihad al-majlis) allows a later correction to be merged with the original testimony. The commentator explains that if a witness, while still in the same judicial session, retracts or corrects part of his testimony, that correction is not treated as a separate statement but as part of the original. This is based on the idea that the entire session is one continuous utterance. The ruling is that such testimony is accepted provided the witness is upright ('adl). This principle is attributed to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, indicating it is a relied-upon position.

Key Terms
المجلس (al-majlis): The session or sitting during which testimony is given in court.
الملحق (al-mulhaq): The correction or additional statement that is attached to the original testimony.
أصل الشهادة (asl al-shahadah): The original testimony given first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In any session
B) Only if the session is the same
C) Only if the correction increases the testimony
D) Only if the correction decreases the testimony

Key Takeaway
A correction to testimony is accepted only if made in the same court session, as it becomes part of the original testimony.

The Principle of Considering Remaining Witnesses on Retraction
States the rule for witness retraction: validity of remaining testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle on witness retraction and apply it to calculate proportional liability.

Core Idea
The retractor bears proportional loss; the remaining testimony validates the claim.

In the chapter of retraction from testimony, a key Hanafi principle determines liability. This lesson explains that consideration is given to the witnesses who maintain their testimony, not to those who retract. Through a concrete example, we see how liability is apportioned when one of two witnesses retracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Read the maxim: 'al-muʿtabar fī bāb al-rujūʿ baqāʾ man baqī lā rujūʿ man rajaʿ'. Understand that the focus is on the remaining witnesses, not the retractors.
This is the core rule for liability.
2
Understand the example
Two witnesses testify against a defendant, the judge awards the property to the plaintiff. Then one witness retracts. How is liability allocated? According to the principle, the retractor pays half the property because the remaining witness still supports half the claim.
The retractor's liability equals the portion no longer supported by a witness.
3
Derive the general rule
Generalize: If multiple witnesses testify and some retract, the right survives proportionally to the number of remaining witnesses. The retractors are liable for the share of the claim that collapses due to their retraction.
This aligns with the idea that repentance matches the nature of the act (open testimony, open retraction).
4
Apply to a new scenario
Consider three witnesses to a debt of 300. One retracts. The remaining two support 200, so the retractor is liable for 100. Confirm using the principle.
The math: total claim divided by original witnesses, retractor's share equals his portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمُعْتَبَرُ فِى بَابِ الرُّجُوعِ بَقَاءُ مَنْ بَقِيَ لَا رُجُوعَ مَنْ رَجَعَ . ترجمہ : گواہی سے رجوع کے باب میں ماچھی گواہوں کا اعتبار ہوگا جنہوں نے رجوع کیا ان کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شہادت سے رجوع کے بعد دیکھا جائے گا کہ اگر تعداد پوری ہے تو پھر رجوع کرنے والوں پر ضمان نہیں آئے گا اور اگر ماضی کی تعداد پوری نہ ہو تو پھر رجوع کرنے والوں پر ضمان آئے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر دو گواہوں نے کسی شخص کے خلاف مال کے بابت گواہی دی اور قاضی نے مدعی کے حق میں فیصلہ کرتے ہوئے اس شخص سے مال لے کر مدعی کے حوالہ کر دیا ، پھر ان دو گواہوں میں سے ایک نے اپنی گواہی سے رجوع کیا تو وہ نصف مال کا ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ مابقی ایک گواہ ہے اور مابقی کا اعتبار ہوتا ہے، لہذا جب ایک اپنی گواہی پر برقرار ہے تو جس نے رجوع کیا ہے اس پر نصف ضمان آئے گا، اور اپنی گواہی پر قائم رہنے والے کی گواہی چونکہ معتبر ہے اس لیے اس پر کوئی ضمان نہ ہوگا۔ والتوبة على حسب الجناية، فالسر بالسر، والإعلان بالإعلان فالشهادة كانت بالإعلان، والرجوع أيضا كذلك. (البناية: كتاب الرجوع عن الشهود، باب كيفية الرجوع عن الشهادة، ج ۹ ص ۲۰۱) الهداية: كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادات، ج ۳ ص ۱۸۲ وَالْعِبْرَةُ فِي الرُّجُوعِ بَقَاء من بَقِى من الشُّهُودَ لَا رُجُوع مِن رَجَعَ وَيبقى الحق يقدر بَقَاءِ الشُّهُود ويتلف بقدر مَا رَجَعَ إِذَا ثَبَت هَذَا الأَصْل تخرج عَلَيْهِ الْمَسَائِل إِذا شهد شَاهِدَانِ على رجل بِمَال وَقضى القَاضِي بذلك وَسلم المال إِلَى الْمُدَّعِي ثُمَّ رَجَعَ أَحدهما غرم نصف المَالِ لِأَنَّهُ بَقِي النِّصْفِ بِبَقَاءِ شَاهد واحد. (تحفة الفقهاء: كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادة، ج ۳ ص ۳۶۵)
The consideration in the chapter of retraction is of the one who remains, not of the one who retracts. Translation: In the chapter of retraction from testimony, the remaining witnesses will be considered; those who retracted will not be considered. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that after retraction from testimony, it will be seen: if the number is complete, then no compensation will be imposed on the retractors; and if the remaining number is not complete, then compensation will be imposed on the retractors. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if two witnesses testified against a person regarding property and the judge decided in favor of the plaintiff, taking the property from that person and handing it over to the plaintiff, then one of the two witnesses retracted his testimony, he would be liable for half the property, because the remaining witness is one, and consideration is given to the remaining one. Therefore, when one remains firm on his testimony, the one who retracts is liable for half the compensation, and the one who remains firm on his testimony is not liable because his testimony is considered. And repentance is according to the crime: secret for secret, open for open. The testimony was open, and the retraction is likewise. (al-Binayah: Book of Retraction from Witnesses, Chapter on How to Retract, vol. 9 p. 201) al-Hidayah: Book of Retraction from Testimonies, vol. 3 p. 182 And the consideration in retraction is of the one who remains from the witnesses, not of the one who retracts. The right remains according to the remaining witnesses and is lost according to what has been retracted. When this principle is established, the issues arise from it: If two witnesses testify against a man regarding property and the judge gives judgment accordingly and hands over the property to the plaintiff, then one of them retracts, he must pay half the property, because half remains due to the remaining single witness. (Tuhfat al-Fuqaha: Book of Retraction from Testimony, vol. 3 p. 365)
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental legal principle (qa'ida) in the chapter of retraction from testimony. The principle states that when witnesses retract their testimony, the consideration is given to those who remain, not to those who retract. The author explains this with a practical example: If two witnesses testify for a plaintiff regarding property and the judge rules accordingly, then one retracts, that retractor is liable for half the property. The remaining witness's testimony still stands and supports half the claim. The passage quotes from al-Binayah, al-Hidayah, and Tuhfat al-Fuqaha to reinforce this principle. The underlying wisdom is that repentance follows the nature of the crime: open retraction corresponds to open testimony. This principle ensures that the testimony of those who remain is still considered valid, and the retractor bears the loss proportionally.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): Retraction by a witness from previously given testimony.
المعتبَر (al-muʿtabar): That which is considered or taken into account.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation; here, the retracting witness must pay the proportional loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two witnesses testify for a property claim of 10,000 dirhams and one retracts, what is the liability according to the principle 'al-muʿtabar fī bāb al-rujūʿ baqāʾ man baqī'?
A) The retracting witness is liable for the full 10,000.
B) The retracting witness is liable for 5,000, and the remaining witness is liable for 5,000.
C) The retracting witness is liable for 5,000, and the remaining witness is not liable.
D) No liability because the judgment becomes void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle on witness retraction holds that the testimony of remaining witnesses remains valid, and the retractor bears liability only for the portion of the claim that is no longer supported.

Compensation Requires Similarity: The Principle of Mumathala
States a legal principle and its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that compensation requires similarity and apply it to cases where the loss is non-material usufruct.

Core Idea
No liability without equivalence: compensation is wajib only when loss and payment are of the same genus.

In Hanafi fiqh, liability for compensation is not automatic whenever a loss occurs. The law demands a precise correspondence between the item lost and the item paid as compensation. This lesson examines that principle through a specific case: witnesses who testify falsely about a marriage and then retract. Because the loss they cause is the usufruct of the sexual organ — a benefit without material equivalent — they face no monetary liability. Understanding this rule prevents misapplication of compensation in cases involving non-property benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Read the opening statement: 'al-tadmin yastadi' al-mumathala' — compensation demands similarity. Recognize that this is a legal maxim governing liability for damages.
The principle sets a condition: without mumathala, compensation is not wajib.
2
Analyze the Condition: What is Similarity?
Mumathala means that the compensatory item must be of the same type or kind as the lost item. For tangible property, this is straightforward; for usufructs and benefits, it may be impossible.
The Hanafi school holds that benefits (manafi‘) do not have equivalents in wealth unless they are explicitly measurable.
3
Apply to the Witness Case
Consider the scenario: witnesses testify that a woman married someone with mahr al-mithl, then retract. The loss inflicted is the usufruct of her sexual organ (manafi‘ al-bud‘). This is a non-material benefit.
Since the loss is not a tangible object, there is no equivalent wealth to serve as compensation.
4
Derive the Ruling
Because the lost usufruct has no similarity (mumathala) with any material payment, no compensation (tadmin) is due from the retracting witnesses. This holds regardless of the stated mahr amount.
The ruling is consistent across multiple Hanafi sources: al-Hidayah, al-Lubab, al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah.
5
Note the Implication for Other Schools
Some non-Hanafi schools may allow compensation for lost usufructs through estimated value. The Hanafi position is stricter: without similarity, no liability.
This ikhtilaf stems from differing definitions of property and compensable loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّضْمِينُ يَسْتَدْعِي الْمُمَاثَلَةَ . ترجمہ: ضامن بنا نا مماثلت کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ضمان کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ جس شے کا انسان ضامن بنایا جائے اس میں اور مضمون بہ میں مماثلت ہو، اگر وہ شے مضمون بہ کی مماثل نہ ہو تو ضمان ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔ صاحب خدا یہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر گواہوں نے کسی عورت کے خلاف گواہی دی کہ اس نے فلاں کے ساتھ مہر مثل پر نکاح کیا ہے، پھر بعد میں گواہوں نے اپنی گواہی سے رجوع کیا تو ان گواہوں پر ضمان نہیں آئے گا، کیونکہ گواہوں نے اپنی گواہی کے ذریعے صرف عورت کے منافع بضعہ کو تلف کیا ہے اور اس کا مثل نہیں ہے، کیونکہ ضمان مماثلت کا تقاضا کرتا ہے، اور مذکورہ صورت میں تلف شدہ منافع بضعہ اور گواہوں کی طرف سے بطورِ ضمان دیا جانے والا مال کے درمیان مماثلت نہیں ، لہذا یہاں بھی رجوع کرنے سے گواہوں پر ضمان نہیں ہوگا۔ عین اور منفعت یعنی منافع بضعہ کے درمیان کوئی مماثلت نہیں ہے۔ قوله وإن رجع أحدهما ضمن النصف والأصل أن المعتبر بقاء من بقى لا رجوع من رجع وقد بقى من يبقى بشهادته نصف الحق. (الجوهرة النيرة كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادة، ج ۲ ص ۲۳۷) (وإن رجع أحدهما ضمن (النصف والأصل أن المعتبر في هذا بقاء من بقي، لا رجوع من رجع، وقد بقى من يتقى بشهادته نصف الحق. اللباب في شرح الكتاب: كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادة، ج ۳ ص ۷۲) الهداية: كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادات ، ج ۳ ص وَإِنْ شَهِدَ عَلَى امْرَأَةٍ بِالنِّكَاحِ ثُمَّ رَجَعَا فَلَا ضَمَانَ عَلَيْهِمَا، سَوَاءٌ كَانَتِ الشَّهَادَةُ بِمِقْدَارِ مَهْرٍ مِثْلِهَا أَوْ بِأَقَلَّ مِنْ ذَلِكَ، لِأَنَّ الْمُتَلَفَ هَاهُنَا مَنَافِعُ الْبُضْعِ وَمَنَافِعُ الْبُضْعِ
Compensation demands similarity. Translation: Making someone liable for compensation requires equivalence. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that for liability it is necessary that there be similarity between the item for which a person is made liable and the compensated item. If that item is not similar to the compensated item, then liability falls. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if witnesses testified against a woman that she married so-and-so with mahr al-mithl, then later the witnesses retracted their testimony, no compensation would be imposed on them, because the witnesses through their testimony only destroyed the woman's usufruct of her sexual organ and there is no equivalent for it, because compensation demands similarity. In the mentioned case, there is no similarity between the destroyed usufruct of the sexual organ and the wealth given as compensation by the witnesses, so here too retraction does not impose liability on the witnesses. There is no similarity between a corporeal item and usufruct, i.e., the usufruct of the sexual organ. (al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah: Book of Retraction from Testimony, vol. 2 p. 237) (al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Book of Retraction from Testimony, vol. 3 p. 72) al-Hidayah: Book of Retraction from Testimonies, vol. 3 p. And if they testify against a woman regarding marriage then retract, there is no compensation upon them, whether the testimony was for the amount of her mahr al-mithl or less than that, because what is destroyed here is the usufruct of the sexual organ and the usufruct of the sexual organ...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents another legal principle: compensation (daman) requires similarity (mumathalah) between what is lost and what is paid as compensation. The author provides a specific application: if witnesses testify that a woman married someone with mahr al-mithl (proper dower), and then retract, they are not liable for compensation because the loss they caused is the usufruct of the woman's sexual organ (manafi' al-bud'), which is not a tangible property and has no exact equivalent. Since compensation demands similarity, and there is no similarity between this usufruct and wealth, no liability arises. The passage references al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah, al-Lubab, and al-Hidayah to confirm this ruling. The principle underscores that liability in cases of retraction applies only when the lost item has a measurable equivalent, such as property.

Key Terms
التضمين (al-taḍmīn): Holding someone liable for compensation or damages.
المماثلة (al-mumāthalah): Equivalence or similarity between the item lost and the compensation paid.
منافع البضع (manāfiʿ al-buḍʿ): The usufruct of the female sexual organ, considered a benefit that cannot be replaced by material wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do witnesses who testify falsely about a marriage and then retract not face compensation according to Hanafi law?
A) Because retraction itself removes liability.
B) Because the loss is the usufruct of the sexual organ, which has no equivalent for compensation.
C) Because the testimony was not accepted by the court.
D) Because the woman is not entitled to compensation for marriage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compensation is only valid when the lost item and the payment share a material similarity; non-material usufructs like manafi' al-bud' have no equivalent and thus attract no liability.
