The Maxim: Indication Does Not Override the Explicit
A concise legal maxim from Kitab al-Hiba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-dalalah la ta'malu fi muqabilati al-sareeh' and apply it to resolve conflicts between explicit statements and implied meanings in gifts and other transactions.

Core Idea
Explicit words always prevail over implied meanings in Hanafi law.

In legal transactions, clarity is paramount. This lesson introduces a fundamental Hanafi maxim from the Book of Gifts: when an explicit statement (sareeh) exists, any implied indication (dalalah) that contradicts it is legally ineffective. This principle ensures that the clear intention expressed by the parties governs, avoiding disputes based on speculative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Begin by committing the Arabic maxim to memory: 'الدلالة لا تعمل في مقابلة الصريح'. Understand its literal meaning: 'Indication does not operate in the face of explicit statement.'
2
Define Key Terms
Identify 'dalalah' as any implied or indicative meaning, and 'sareeh' as a clear, unambiguous statement. Recognize that this maxim establishes a hierarchy: the explicit governs over the implicit.
In Hanafi usul, 'sareeh' is preferred because it comes directly from the words themselves.
3
Apply to a Gift Scenario
Consider a case where a donor explicitly says 'I give you this as a gift' but circumstances suggest it was a loan. The maxim dictates that the explicit statement prevails; the gift is valid regardless of contrary indications.
This ensures legal certainty and protects parties from hidden intentions.
4
Recognize the Scope
Understand that this maxim applies broadly in contracts, gifts, wills, and other transactions. It is a tool to resolve conflicts between literal text and inferred meanings.
It does not deny the role of dalalah in interpreting ambiguous texts, only when it directly opposes the explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة (١٦) اَلدَّلَالَةُ لَا تَعْمَلُ فِى مُقَابَلَةِ الصَّرِيح . ترجمہ: دلالت صریح کے مقابلہ میں عمل نہیں کرتی ۔
Book of Gift (16) Indication does not operate in the face of the explicit. Translation: Indication does not work in opposition to explicit statement.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This is a concise legal maxim from Kitab al-Hiba (Book of Gift) in the Hidayah. It states that when there is an explicit statement (sareeh), any implicit indication (dalalah) that contradicts it is ineffective. In other words, clear wording takes precedence over implied meanings. This principle is used in Hanafi fiqh to resolve conflicts between the literal text and inferred intentions, particularly in contracts and gifts. For example, if a donor explicitly says 'I give this as a gift,' any indication that it might be a loan or something else is disregarded. This principle ensures legal certainty.

Key Terms
دَلَالَة (Dalalah): Indication or implied meaning, as opposed to explicit text.
صَرِيح (Sareeh): Explicit, clear statement with unambiguous meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the maxim 'al-dalalah la ta'malu fi muqabilati al-sareeh' mean?
A) Implied meanings are always preferred over explicit statements.
B) Explicit statements cannot be used to interpret gifts.
C) Indication does not operate in the face of explicit statement.
D) Indication and explicit statement are equally valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an explicit statement (sareeh) overrides any contradictory implied meaning (dalalah), ensuring legal clarity and certainty.

Borrower May Relend a Non-Consumable Borrowed Item
Explaining that a borrower can relend the borrowed item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a borrower lending a borrowed item and explain the qiyas with the bequest of service case.

Core Idea
Borrower may relend if item does not change with use, because 'ariyah transfers ownership of usufruct.

This lesson examines a specific permission in Hanafi law: a borrower (musta'ir) may lend the borrowed item to a third party, but only if the item does not vary with use. The Hanafis derive this from the nature of 'ariyah as tamlik al-manafi', analogizing it to the bequest of a slave's service. Understanding this ruling clarifies the scope of the borrower's rights and the limits set by the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The borrower lending the borrowed item to another person. The ruling is permissible (mubah) under a condition.
The act is called i'arah (lending) by the musta'ir.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is qiyas (analogy) with the case of wasiyyah bi al-khidmah (bequest of service of a slave). In that case, the legatee can transfer the service to another because he owns the usufruct. The same applies here.
The qiyas is based on the shared illa: tamlik al-manafi'.
3
Trace the illa
The effective cause (illa) is the transfer of ownership of usufruct (tamlik al-manafi'). Since 'ariyah grants ownership of usufruct, the borrower becomes owner of the benefits. Ownership includes the power to transfer to others.
This illa is explicit: 'ariyah is tamlik al-manafi'.
4
Apply the condition
The borrower may only relend if the item does not change or differ with use. Examples: land for cultivation, house for residence, animal for loading. If the item changes (e.g., food, fuel), relending is not allowed because the usufruct is not uniform.
Condition: mimma la yakhtalif bi-ikhtilaf al-musta'mil.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require explicit permission from the owner. The Hanafi position relies on the nature of 'ariyah as ownership, not mere permission.
The practical consequence: Hanafis allow relending without express permission, others do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مستعیر شے مستعار کو آگے عاریت پر دے سکتا ہے، بشرطیکہ وہ چیز استعمال سے بدلتی نہ ہو اور مختلف بھی نہ ہوتی ہو، مثلاً جانور پر بوجھ لادنا، زمین میں کاشت کرنا ، مکان میں سکونت اپنانا وغیرہ، کیونکہ عاریت تملیک منافع کا نام ہے اور تملیک میں متملک آگے مالک بنا سکتا ہے، جیسے کہ اگر کسی نے کسی کے لیے اپنے غلام کی خدمت کی وصیت کی تو موصی لہ بالخدمت اس غلام کو دوسرے کی خدمت کے لیے دے سکتا ہے، کیونکہ یہ منافع کی تملیک ہے، بالکل ایسے ہی عاریت میں بھی مستعبیر آگے تملیک منافع یعنی منافع کا مالک بنا سکتا ہے۔
وَلَنَا أَنَّهَا تَمُلِيكُ الْمَنَافِعِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ فَيَتَضَمَّنُ مِثْلَهُ كَالْمُوصَى لَهُ بِالْخِدْمَةِ جَازَ أَنْ يُعِيرَ لِتَمَلْكِهِ الْمَنْفَعَةَ. (العناية: كتاب العارية، ج ۹ ص ١٠)
(قَوْلُهُ: وَلَهُ أَنْ يُعِيرَهُ إِذَا كَانَ مِمَّا لَا يَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْمُسْتَعْمِلِ) قَالَ عَامَّةُ الشَّرَّاحِ كَالْحَمْلِ وَالاسْتِخْدَامِ وَالسُّكْنَى وَالزَّرَاعَةِ.
(فتح القدير : كتاب العارية، ج ۹ ص ١٠)
(وله) أى لـلـمـعير (أن يعيره إذا كان مما لا يختلف باختلاف المستعمل)..... ونحن نقول هو تملیک المنافع على ما ذکرنا یعنی فی هذا الباب فیملک الإعارة) يعني إذا كانت الإعارة تمليكا للمنافع يملك المستعير حينئذ الإعارة، لأن الشيء يتضمن مثله ( كالموصى له بالخدمة) أى بخدمة عبد مثلا يجوز له أن يعيره لتملك المنفعة. (البناية: كتاب العارية، ج ۱۰ ص ۱۴۸ )
الهداية كتاب الهبة، ج ۳ ص ۲۸۴
The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is stating that the borrower (musta'ir) can give the borrowed item further on loan ('ariyah), provided that the item does not change with use and is not different, e.g., loading an animal, cultivating land, residing in a house, etc., because 'ariyah is the transfer of ownership of usufruct (tamlik al-manafi'), and in tamlik, the owner can make (someone else) owner, just as if someone bequeaths the service of his slave to someone, the legatee of the service (musa lahu bil-khidmah) can give that slave for service to another, because it is tamlik of usufruct. Exactly like this, in 'ariyah, the borrower can further transfer the ownership of usufruct, i.e., he can make (someone else) owner of the usufruct. And for us (Hanafis), it (the permissibility) is because it ('ariyah) is tamlik al-manafi' as has passed, so it includes its like, like the one who is bequeathed service, it is permissible for him to lend (the slave) because he owns the usufruct. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-'Ariyah, vol. 9, p. 10) (His statement: 'And he may lend it if it is of the type that does not differ with the difference of the user' — the generality of commentators said: like loading, service, residence, and cultivation.) (Fath al-Qadir: Kitab al-'Ariyah, vol. 9, p. 10) (And he [the lender] may lend it if it is of the type that does not differ with the difference of the user... And we say: it is tamlik al-manafi' as we mentioned i.e., in this chapter, so he owns the right to lend. That is, if 'ariyah is tamlik of usufruct, then the borrower owns the right to lend, because a thing includes its like, like the one bequeathed service, i.e., for example the service of a slave, it is permissible for him to lend him to own the usufruct. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-'Ariyah, vol. 10, p. 148) Al-Hidayah Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 3, p. 284
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the permissibility of a borrower (musta'ir) lending the borrowed item to a third party. The author of Al-Hidayah explains that this is allowed only if the item does not change or differ with use, such as loading an animal, cultivating land, or living in a house. The underlying reason is that 'ariyah (loan) is a transfer of ownership of usufruct (tamlik al-manafi'). In Hanafi law, when one owns the usufruct of an item, one may transfer that usufruct to another, just as a legatee of a slave's service can transfer that service to another person. This is established by qiyas (analogical reasoning) with the case of bequest of service (wasiyyah bi al-khidmah). The Arabic quotes from Al-'Inayah, Fath al-Qadir, and Al-Binayah confirm this ruling. The key condition is that the item must not vary with the user; otherwise, the second lending would not be permissible because the usufruct is not uniform.

Key Terms
مستعير (musta'ir): The borrower (one who receives an item on loan for use).
عارية ('ariyah): A loan of an item for its use without compensation; the owner allows use of the item temporarily.
تمليك المنافع (tamlik al-manafi'): Transfer of ownership of usufruct: the person receiving the item becomes the owner of the right to use it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In all cases, because 'ariyah transfers full ownership.
B) Only if the item does not change or differ with use.
C) Only if the lender explicitly permits in the contract.
D) Only for consumable items like food.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a borrower to relend a borrowed item that does not change with use, based on the transfer of ownership of usufruct (tamlik al-manafi').

Indication Does Not Operate Against Explicit Statement
Explaining the principle that dalalah is overridden by sarih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal principle that indication (dalalah) does not operate against an explicit statement (sarih) and apply it to determine the validity of possession in a gift contract.

Core Idea
Explicit prohibition overrides implied permission – dalalah is void against sarih.

In Hanafi law, actions often carry implicit meanings. A donor's offer to give a gift implies permission to take possession. But what happens if the donor explicitly forbids it? This lesson explores the hierarchy of legal expressions: explicit speech (sarih) trumps implied indication (dalalah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
The act is taking possession of a gift (qabd al-hibah). The question is: is it valid when the donor has explicitly forbidden it?
2
Recognize the two opposing indications
The donor's offer (ijab) implies permission to take possession (dalalah). The donor's explicit statement 'Do not take possession' is a sarih prohibition.
Dalalah is an inferred meaning from context; sarih is a direct, unambiguous statement.
3
Apply the principle that sarih overrides dalalah
According to the principle 'dalalah la ta'mal fi muqabilat al-sarih', explicit statement takes precedence. Therefore, the explicit prohibition cancels the implied permission.
This is a core usul principle applied in fiqh.
4
Determine the ruling
Since the explicit prohibition overrides, any possession taken after the prohibition is invalid, even if it is within the same assembly.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the donor previously permitted by implication.
5
Understand the hypothetical objection
A hypothetical objection asks: how can possession be invalid when the enabling (taslit) from the offer still exists? The answer is that the explicit prohibition nullifies the indicated permission.
This demonstrates the strength of sarih over dalalah.

تشريح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جہاں صراحت ہو، اس کے مقابلے میں دلالت کو عمل نہیں دیا جائے گا، بلکہ صراحت کو بروئے کار لایا جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر موہوب لہ نے شے موہو بہ کو واہب کی اجازت کے بغیر مجلس میں قبضہ کیا تو یہ استحسانا جائز ہے، لیکن اگر واہب نے منع کیا ، پھر قبضہ کیا تو یہ قبضہ درست نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ موہوبہ شے کے قبضہ کرنے کی اجازت واہب کی طرف سے دلاتا تھی، لیکن جب اس نے منع کیا تو اس کی طرف سے منع کی صراحت ہو گئی اور صریح کے مقابلہ میں دلالت عمل نہیں کرتی ، لہذا یہاں بھی صراحتاً روکنے کے بعد دلالتا اجازت عمل نہیں کرے گی ۔

(البناية: كتاب الهبة، ج ۱۰ ص ۱۶۴)

Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that where there is an explicit statement (sarih), indication (dalalah) will not be given effect in its face; rather the explicit statement will be applied. The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning the said principle, is proving that if the donee (mawhub lahu) takes possession (qabd) of the gifted item in the assembly without the donor's permission, it is permissible by istihsan. But if the donor forbids (explicitly), then afterwards possession is not valid, because the permission to take possession was indicated by the donor's offer (ijab), but when he forbids, there is an explicit prohibition, and indication does not operate against the explicit. So here too, after explicit forbiddance, the indication of permission will not operate. (17) Non-existent thing is not a subject for ownership. (Translation of Arabic) If it is said: This entails the case when he forbids possession, while the enabling (taslit) exists, yet possession is not permissible for him. He answered: 'Unlike when he forbids him,' meaning explicitly in the assembly, because indication does not operate against the explicit. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 9, p. 23) (Unlike when he forbids him... and this is an answer to a hypothetical question: 'It follows from this that when he forbids possession, while the enabling exists, possession is not allowed.' The explanation of the answer is that when he forbids him, meaning explicitly about possession in the assembly by saying 'do not take possession,' then his possession is not valid in the assembly or after it, because indication does not operate against the explicit.) By 'dalalah' is meant the permission derived from the donor's offer (ijab) of possession, and by 'sarih' is meant his statement 'do not take possession,' for the permission derived from the offer is an indication that is nullified by the existence of explicit prohibition, because indication does not stand against the explicit. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 10, p. 164) Al-Hidayah Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 3, p. 288
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines and applies a key usul principle: 'Indication (dalalah) does not operate against an explicit statement (sarih).' The author explains that when there is explicit wording, any implicit or inferred permission is overridden. The specific application here is in the context of gifts (hibah). In Hanafi fiqh, if the donor makes an offer (ijab) of a gift, this implicitly permits the donee to take possession in the same assembly (by istihsan). However, if the donor explicitly says 'do not take possession,' that explicit prohibition cancels the implied permission, and any subsequent possession is invalid. This is because the implied permission (dalalah) is weaker than explicit speech. The Arabic quotes from Al-'Inayah and Al-Binayah reinforce this by addressing a hypothetical objection: how can possession be invalid when the donor's offer (taslit) is still present? The answer is that the explicit prohibition overrides the implied permission. The passage also briefly introduces another principle: 'Non-existent thing is not a subject for ownership' (al-ma'dum laysa bi-mahall lil-milk), but this is only stated without further elaboration here.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalalah): Indication or implication derived from the context or from an action; here it refers to the implied permission to take possession arising from the donor's offer.
صريح (sarih): Explicit, clear statement (as opposed to implicit). Here it refers to the donor's explicit command 'do not take possession.'
موهوب له (mawhub lahu): The donee, the recipient of a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is invalid because the explicit prohibition overrides the implied permission.
C) It is valid only if the donee waits for a new offer.
D) It is invalid only if the donor says 'I forbid you.'

Key Takeaway
Explicit speech (sarih) always outweighs implied meaning (dalalah) in Hanafi legal interpretation.

Explicit Prohibition Overrides Implicit Permission in Gift Possession
Establishing that explicit prohibition nullifies implied permission in hibah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that explicit prohibition overrides implicit permission in the context of gift possession (hibah) and determine the validity of possession.

Core Idea
Explicit prohibition nullifies implied permission — the donor's 'do not take possession' overrides any implicit consent.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle that governs the validity of possession in gifts. When a donor offers a gift, there is an implied permission to take possession. However, if the donor explicitly prohibits possession in the same gathering, that explicit statement overrides the implication, rendering possession invalid. We will explore the rationale and the ruling as presented in al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is the act being ruled upon? It is the donee's taking possession of the gifted item after the donor explicitly prohibits it. The ruling is that such possession is invalid (batil).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the established Hanafi usul principle: 'al-dalalah la ta'mal fi muqabil al-sarih' (implicit indication does not operate in the face of explicit statement). This rational principle is the basis.
The dalil is aqli (rational) derived from the priority of explicit over implicit in contractual matters.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The donor's explicit prohibition ('do not take possession') is sarih — clear and unambiguous. The permission implied from the offer (ijab) is dalalah — an inferred meaning. Since sarih is stronger, it invalidates the dalalah, so possession under explicit prohibition fails to transfer ownership.
The text emphasizes that the prohibition must be explicit and in the same gathering.
4
Note the condition and its effect
The key condition is the presence of an explicit verbal prohibition. If the prohibition is implicit or ambiguous, the original implied permission may still be effective. The effect is that possession becomes invalid (batil), and the gift is not completed.
5
Evaluate the consequence for fatwa
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that any possession after explicit prohibition in the same gathering is invalid. The donee does not acquire ownership, and the gift remains with the donor.
This ruling is consistent across Zahir al-Riwayah.

Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that where there is an explicit statement, implicit indication will not be given effect; rather, the explicit statement will be acted upon. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that if the donee takes possession of the gifted item in the gathering without the donor's permission, it is permissible by istihsan, but if the donor prohibits and then possession is taken, that possession is invalid, because the permission to take possession was indicated by the donor, but when he prohibited, the prohibition became explicit, and in the face of an explicit statement, implicit indication is not operative. Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a usul principle from al-Hidayah, dealing with the interplay between explicit (sarih) and implicit (dalalah) evidence in the context of gift (hibah). The author explains that the principle is: where there is explicit text, implicit indication is not given effect. He applies this to a scenario in hibah: if the donor implicitly permits possession by making the offer, but then explicitly prohibits possession in the gathering, the explicit prohibition overrides the implicit permission. The passage then presents a hypothetical objection: if we say that implicit permission suffices for valid possession, then what about the case where the donor prohibits? The granting of authority (tasleet) exists, yet possession is not valid? The answer is that the explicit prohibition (the donor's words 'do not take possession') overrides any implicit permission, because indication does not operate in the face of explicit text. The commentators confirm that the permission from the offer is a mere indication that is nullified by the explicit prohibition. This ruling is based on the recognized Hanafi principle that dalalah (implicit meaning) is weaker than sarih (explicit wording) and cannot resist it.

Key Terms
صراحت (sarih): Explicit statement or clear wording; in this text, the donor's direct prohibition 'do not take possession'.
دلالت (dalalah): Implicit indication or inferred meaning; here, the permission implied by the donor's offer of possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Valid because the offer implies permission.
B) Invalid because explicit prohibition overrides implicit permission.
C) Valid only if the donee is a relative.
D) Invalid only if the prohibition is before the offer.

Key Takeaway
Explicit prohibition by the donor nullifies the implied permission from the gift offer, making possession invalid and the gift incomplete.

Non-Existent Object Cannot Be Owned: Application in Hibah
Applying the principle that non-existent things cannot be owned to invalidate future-property gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle 'al-ma'dum laysa bi mahallin lil milk' and apply it to determine the validity of gifting future property.

Core Idea
A gift of flour not yet milled is void — non-existent property cannot be owned.

The Hanafi school of fiqh holds a firm distinction between what exists and what is merely potential. This lesson examines a foundational principle that a non-existent thing cannot be a valid subject of ownership. We will see how this applies to gifts of future property, such as flour in wheat or oil in sesame, and why even later delivery cannot cure the initial invalidity of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is gifting a non-existent object (e.g., flour in wheat). The ruling in Hanafi is that the gift is batil (void).
The ruling is not merely fasid but batil because the subject matter is legally incapable of being owned.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the principle 'al-ma'dum laysa bi mahallin lil milk' (the non-existent is not a locus for ownership).
This principle is a rational axiom (dalil aqli) derived from the nature of ownership contracts.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The principle directly states that a non-existent thing cannot be owned. Since the gifted object does not exist at the time of the contract, the contract is void.
Existence is a necessary condition for the mahall (locus) of ownership.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow such gifts under certain conditions (e.g., if the object is determinable and will exist), but Hanafi strictly requires actual existence at contract time.
The difference stems from the definition of mahall al-milk and the scope of salam.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi within the school, supported by the fundamental principle and consistent across Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists on this point; the ruling is authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ٧ ١ ١ ) الْمَعْدُومُ لَيْسَ بِمَحَلَّ لِلْمِلْكِ. ترجمہ: معدوم چیز محل ملک نہیں ہے۔

اصول ہدایہ ثالث
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو چیز معدوم ہو وہ ملک کا محل نہیں بن سکتی یعنی انسان معدوم چیز کا مالک نہیں بن سکتا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر واہب نے کہا کہ اس گندم میں جو آتا ہے وہ میں تجھے ہبہ کرتا ہوں ، یا اس تل میں جو تیل ہے وہ میں تمہیں ہبہ کرتا ہوں تو ہبہ باطل ہے، کیونکہ یہاں موہوب یعنی آٹا اور تیل معدوم ہیں اور معدوم چیز حل ملک نہیں ہے اور جو چیز محل ملک نہ ہو اس کا یہ فاسد ہوتا ہے، لہذا گندم میں آنے کو ہبہ کرنا بھی فاسد ہوگا ، یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص گندم اور تل غصب کر کے آٹا اور تیل نکال لے تو وہ مغصوب کا ضامن ہو کر ان چیزوں کا مالک ہو جاتا ہے، کیونکہ غصب کے وقت یہ چیزیں موجود نہیں تھیں ، اس لیے ان پر فعل غصب واقع نہیں ہوا۔

قوله ولو) وهب دقيقا فى حنطة أو دهنا في سمسم فالهبة فاسدة فإن طحن وسلم لم يجز لأن الموهوب معدوم والمعدوم ليس بمحل للملك فوقع العقد باطلا فلا ينعقد إلا بالتجديد الجوهرة النيرة كتاب الهبة، ج ١ ص ٣٢٦) (قال) أى القدورى ولو) وهب دقيقا فى حنطة أو دهنا في سمسم فالهبة فاسدة) الأصل هنا أن المحل إذا كان معدوما حالة العقد لم ينعقد إلا بالتجديد. البناية: كتاب الهبة، ج ١٠ ص ۱۷۴)
ولو وهب دقيقاً فى حنطة أو دناً في سمسم) أو سمنا في لبن (فالهبة فاسدة) أى باطلة، ولذا قال: (فإن طحن الحنطة (وسلم) الدقيق، أو أخرج الدهن من السمسم أو السمن من اللبن، وسلم للموهوب له لم يجز ذلك، لأن الموهوب معدوم والمعدوم ليس محلا للملك فوقع العقد باطلا، فلا ينعقد إلا بالتحديد. (اللباب : كتاب الهبة، ج ۲ ص ۱۷۲)
(7.11) The non-existent is not a locus for ownership. Translation: A non-existent thing is not a place for ownership. Principles of Hidayah Volume 3. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that a thing which is non-existent cannot become a locus of ownership, i.e., a person cannot become owner of a non-existent thing. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the donor says 'I gift you the flour that is in this wheat,' or 'I gift you the oil that is in this sesame,' then the gift is invalid, because the gifted object (flour and oil) is non-existent, and a non-existent thing is not a locus of ownership, and whatever is not a locus of ownership, its contract is corrupt. Therefore, gifting the flour in wheat will also be corrupt. This is the reason that if someone usurps wheat and sesame and extracts flour and oil, he becomes the guarantor of the usurped items and becomes their owner, because at the time of usurpation these things were not existent, hence the act of usurpation did not occur upon them. His statement: 'And if he gifts flour in wheat or oil in sesame, then the gift is corrupt; if he grinds it and delivers, it is not permissible because the gifted object is non-existent, and the non-existent is not a locus for ownership, so the contract occurred void, and it is only effective by renewal.' (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 1, p. 326) (He said, i.e., al-Quduri: 'And if he gifts flour in wheat or oil in sesame, the gift is corrupt.') The principle here is that if the locus is non-existent at the time of contract, it is not effective except by renewal. (Al-Binayah, Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 10, p. 174) 'And if he gifts flour in wheat or oil in sesame or ghee in milk, the gift is corrupt, i.e., void, and therefore he said: If he grinds the wheat and delivers the flour, or extracts the oil from sesame or ghee from milk, and delivers to the donee, it is not valid, because the gifted object is non-existent and the non-existent is not a locus for ownership, so the contract occurred void, and it is only effective by renewal.' (Al-Lubab, Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 2, p. 172)
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational Hanafi principle: 'al-ma'dum laysa bi mahallin lil milk' (the non-existent is not a valid subject for ownership). The author explains that this means one cannot own something that does not exist at the time of the transaction. He applies this to hibah (gift): if someone gifts something that is potential but not yet actual (like flour in wheat, oil in sesame, or ghee in milk), the gift is fāsid (corrupt) because the object (the flour, oil, etc.) is non-existent. Even if later the wheat is ground and the flour is delivered, the gift remains invalid because it was void from the start; a new contract (tajdīd) is required. The passage also mentions a secondary application: if a person usurps (ghasb) wheat and sesame, then extracts flour and oil, he becomes liable for the original wheat/sesame but owns the flour/oil because they did not exist at the time of usurpation. This illustrates the same principle from a different angle. The text quotes several Hanafi commentaries (al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, al-Binayah, al-Lubab) to confirm the ruling.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum): A thing that does not exist at the time of the contract; potential or future property
محل ملک (mahall al-milk): The locus or subject of ownership; that which can be legally owned

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person says to another, 'I gift you the flour that will be from this wheat.' According to the Hanafi principle of al-ma'dum, this gift is:
A) Valid if the wheat is ground later.
B) Valid because the wheat exists.
C) Invalid because the flour is non-existent at the time of contract.
D) Valid if the donor specifies the quantity.

Key Takeaway
A non-existent thing cannot be a valid subject of ownership in Hanafi law; a gift of future property is void ab initio and requires a new contract upon creation.

The Maxim of Prevention According to the Preventer (Imtina' bi Qadr al-Mani') and Revocation of Gift
States a maxim and its application to gift revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-imtina' bi qadr al-mani' and apply it to determine the extent of revocation of a gift when the donee has alienated part of the gifted property.

Core Idea
A donor's right to revoke a gift is only barred in the portion where the donee has alienated the property.

In Hanafi law, a donor can generally revoke a gift unless there is a legal preventer (mani'). One such preventer is when the donee sells the gifted property. But what if the donee sells only part? Does the entire gift become irrevocable or only the sold part? This lesson explores the elegant Hanafi maxim that provides a proportional answer: prohibition is only as extensive as the preventer itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim
Recognize the Arabic maxim 'الامتناع بقدر المانع' (al-imtina' bi qadr al-mani'). This is the central legal principle being introduced.
2
Understand the Meaning
The maxim means that the prohibition (imtina') of exercising a right corresponds exactly to the extent of the preventer (mani'). In the context of gift revocation, the preventer is the donee's alienation of the gifted property.
3
Apply to Partial Alienation
Consider a scenario where the donee sells half of an undivided gifted house. The preventer exists only in that half. Therefore, revocation is prohibited only for the sold half, and permitted for the unsold half.
The donor retains the right to revoke the unsold portion.
4
Recognize the Proof
The proof for this ruling is rational (dalil aqli): the right of revocation is a legal entitlement, and its prohibition should be coterminous with the obstacle that blocks it. This is a form of qiyas or talil based on the nature of preventers.
No specific Quranic or Hadith text is cited; it is derived from general legal reasoning.
5
Note the Implication
If the donee has not alienated any part, the donor can revoke the entire gift. If the donee alienates the entire property, revocation is completely prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

باب ما يصح رجوعه وما لا يصح
(۱۱۸) اَلْاِمْتِنَاعُ بِقَدْرِ الْمَانِعِ. 0 ترجمہ: امتناع بقدر مانع ہوتا ہے۔
۱۶۹
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ممنوع ہونا مانع کی بقدر ہوتا ہے، یعنی جس مقدار میں مانع پایا جائے اتنی مقدار میں رجوع کرنا منع ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا ر ہے ہیں کہ اگر موہوب لہ نے نصف مثلا موہوب غیر مقسوم مکان کو بیچ دیا تو واہب باقی نصف میں رجوع کر سکتا ہے، کیونکہ رجوع سے منع صرف اس مقدار میں ہے جس میں مانع پایا جائے اور یہاں مانع نصف میں (جس کو موہوب بیچ چکا ہے) پایا جاتا ہے ، لہذار جوع بھی صرف نصف میں منع ہوگا، باقی نصف میں رجوع کر سکتا ہے۔ اور اگر موہوب نے اُسے فروخت نہیں کیا تو کل میں بھی رجوع کر سکتا ہے۔
Prevention is according to the extent of the preventer. Translation: Prevention is to the extent of the preventer. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that being prohibited is according to the extent of the preventer, meaning that to the extent that the preventer is found, returning is prohibited to that extent. The author of Hidayah, mentioning this principle, informs that if the donee, for example, sells half of the undivided gifted property, then the donor can revoke the remaining half, because prohibition of revocation is only to the extent that the preventer is present. Here the preventer is present in the half (which the donee has sold), so revocation is prohibited only in that half; he can revoke the other half. And if the donee did not sell it at all, then he can revoke the entire gift.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qawida fiqhiyya) and its application. The passage type is ruling with proof. The maxim 'الامتناع بقدر المانع' (prevention is according to the extent of the preventer) is a general principle in Hanafi fiqh. Here it is applied to the revocation of a gift (hiba). In Hanafi law, a donor may revoke a gift unless a preventer (mani) exists. One such preventer is the donee's alienation of the gifted property. If the donee has sold part of the property, the donor's right to revoke is limited to the unsold portion. The explanation clarifies that the prohibition of revocation corresponds exactly to the portion of the gift that has been alienated. If the donee sells half of an undivided property, the donor cannot revoke that half but may revoke the remaining half. If no sale occurred, the donor may revoke the entire gift. This ruling is found in the Hidayah under the chapter on what permits and prevents revocation of gift.

Key Terms
الامتناع (al-imtina'): Legal prohibition or prevention; here it refers to the prohibition of revocation of a gift.
المانع (al-mani'): A legal preventer that bars the exercise of a right; here specifically the donee's sale of the gifted property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the gift is one property.
B) No, because the donor can only revoke the unsold half.
C) Yes, because the donee's act is a breach.
D) No, because revocation is prohibited entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-imtina' bi qadr al-mani' ensures that a donor's right to revoke a gift is only barred in the exact proportion that the donee has alienated the property.

The Principle of Exception in Contracts: When Istithna Is Void
States and applies the maxim to a gift case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on exception and apply it to the gift of a pregnant slave girl to determine the validity of the gift and exception.

Core Idea
Exception is valid only where the contract itself could be validly made on the excepted item.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi contract maxim: an exception (istithna) is only effective if the excepted item can itself be the subject of a valid contract. We will see how this principle resolves a classic case: a donor gifts a pregnant slave girl but tries to exclude the unborn child. Understanding this maxim prevents confusion about what is included in a gift when an exception is made.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
The maxim 'الاستثناء لا يعمل إلا في محل يعمل فيه العقد' means 'Exception does not operate except in a place where the contract operates.' This is the core rule.
Memorize the Arabic formulation.
2
Identify the case
The case is: a donor (wahib) gifts a pregnant slave girl (jariya) and says 'except her pregnancy' (illa hamlaha).
3
Determine whether the excepted item can stand alone as a contract subject
Ask: Can a contract (e.g., sale or gift) be validly concluded on the pregnancy alone? In Hanafi law, an unborn child is not a separate property; it cannot be the subject of a standalone contract.
Compare with a condition like excepting a known object (e.g., 'except the furniture in the house') which can be separately contracted.
4
Apply the maxim
Since the pregnancy cannot be a standalone contract subject, the exception is placed in a 'place' where the contract does not operate. Therefore, the exception is void (batil).
The exception fails, so it is treated as a void condition (shart fasid).
5
Conclude the ruling
In Hanafi law, a gift is not invalidated by a void condition (unlike sale). Thus the gift remains valid (sahih) and includes the pregnancy; the donee owns both the mother and unborn child.
The Shafi‘i school disagrees: they treat the exception as valid, so the gift excludes the pregnancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author of Hidayah, mentioning this principle, informs that if the donor gifted a pregnant slave girl and excepted her pregnancy, then the gift is valid and the exception is void, meaning that both the slave girl and the pregnancy belong to the donee, because in the mentioned case the donor made an exception of the pregnancy, and a contract on the pregnancy alone is not valid, so the exception of the pregnancy is also not valid, and when the exception is not valid, it becomes a void condition, and the gift is not invalidated by void conditions. Know that the exception of pregnancy is of three types: a type where the contract is valid and the exception is void; a type where both are void; and a type where both are valid. The first is our case in gift, marriage, khul', and reconciliation for blood money of intentional killing, for if he gifted the slave girl except her pregnancy, the gift is valid and the exception is void, because exception does not operate except in a locus where the contract operates. Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the legal principle that an exception (istithna) in a contract is only effective if the contract could have been validly concluded on the excepted item alone. The passage type is ruling with proof. The maxim 'الاستثناء لا يعمل إلا في محل يعمل فيه العقد' is applied to a specific case: a donor gifts a pregnant slave girl but excepts the pregnancy. Hanafi law rules that the gift is valid and the exception is void, because a contract on the pregnancy alone (as a separate item) is invalid. The exception is treated as a void condition, and since a gift is not invalidated by void conditions, the entire slave girl including the pregnancy passes to the donee. The commentary from Al-Ikhtiyar elaborates that this ruling is one of three categories of exception of pregnancy: (1) contract valid, exception void (gift, marriage, khul', and reconciliation for intentional killing), (2) both void (e.g., sale of a pregnant animal with exception of its unborn), and (3) both valid (e.g., bequest). The key rationale is that the exception cannot operate where the contract itself would not be valid on the excepted item.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exclusion of part of a thing from a contract; here, excluding the pregnancy from a gift of a slave girl.
الشرط الفاسد (al-shart al-fasid): A condition that is void (fasid) because it is not permissible in the contract; here, the condition of excepting the pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Hanafi law rule the exception of pregnancy void when gifting a pregnant slave girl?
A) Because the pregnancy is unknown (jahalah).
B) Because a contract cannot be validly made on the pregnancy alone.
C) Because the donor intended to deceive.
D) Because the gift of a slave girl with excepted pregnancy is a sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an exception is valid only if the excluded item can itself be the subject of a valid contract; otherwise the exception is void but does not invalidate the gift.

Three Categories of Exception of Pregnancy in Hanafi Law
Classifies the exception of pregnancy into three categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the exception of pregnancy into three categories and state the ruling for each.

Core Idea
The exception of pregnancy has three categories: contract valid/exception void, both void, both valid.

Al-Ikhtiyar expands on the exception of pregnancy by categorizing it into three distinct scenarios. This lesson details each category: where the contract is valid and the exception void (gift, marriage, khul', and sulh on intentional blood money), where both are void (sale of a pregnant animal with exception of unborn), and where both are valid (bequest). Understanding this classification helps in predicting the legal outcome in different contracts.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three categories from Al-Ikhtiyar
Al-Ikhtiyar states: 'Know that the exception of pregnancy is of three types: a type where the contract is valid and the exception is void; a type where both are void; and a type where both are valid.'
2
List the contracts in the first category
First category: contract valid, exception void. It applies to gift (hiba), marriage (nikah), khul', and sulh (reconciliation) for intentional killing. For these, the exception of pregnancy is void, and the contract includes the pregnancy.
The principle 'exception operates only where the contract operates' applies.
3
Identify the second category
Second category: both contract and exception are void. This occurs in sale of a pregnant animal (e.g., sheep) with exception of its unborn. Since sale is commutative and the subject must be known, the exception causes uncertainty (gharar) rendering the sale void. The exception is also void but that does not save the contract.
The text does not elaborate but this is standard Hanafi rule.
4
Identify the third category
Third category: both contract and exception are valid. This occurs in bequest (wasiyya). If a person bequeaths a pregnant slave girl except her pregnancy, the bequest is valid and the exception is valid, so the legatee receives only the mother; the unborn stays with the testator's estate.
Bequest is a transfer of ownership after death; the donor can impose conditions more freely.
5
Review the rationale
The rationale for the first category is the maxim. For sale, the exception introduces uncertainty (gharar) which invalidates the sale. For bequest, the testator's discretion is wider, so both are valid.

Source Text

(ruler returns to the rest, because prevention is according to the extent of the preventer). Al-Binayah: Book of Gift, chapter on what is valid for revocation and what is not, vol.10 p.194. The author of Hidayah, mentioning this principle, informs that if the donor gifted a pregnant slave girl and excepted her pregnancy, then the gift is valid and the exception is void, meaning that both the slave girl and the pregnancy belong to the donee, because in the mentioned case the donor made an exception of the pregnancy, and a contract on the pregnancy alone is not valid, so the exception of the pregnancy is also not valid, and when the exception is not valid, it becomes a void condition, and the gift is not invalidated by void conditions. Al-Ikhtiyar li-Ta'leel al-Mukhtar: Book of Gift, vol.3 p.50: He said: 'And whoever gifts a slave girl except her pregnancy...' Know that the exception of pregnancy is of three types: a type where the contract is valid and the exception is void; a type where both are void; and a type where both are valid. The first is our case in gift, marriage, khul', and reconciliation for blood money of intentional killing, for if he gifted the slave girl except her pregnancy, the gift is valid and the exception is void, because exception does not operate except in a locus where the contract operates. Al-Inayah: Book of Gift, chapter on and whoever gifts a slave girl except her pregnancy, vol.9 p.50. Hidayah: Book of Gift, chapter on what is valid for revocation and what is not, vol.3 p.294.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the legal principle that an exception (istithna) in a contract is only effective if the contract could have been validly concluded on the excepted item alone. The passage type is ruling with proof. The maxim 'الاستثناء لا يعمل إلا في محل يعمل فيه العقد' is applied to a specific case: a donor gifts a pregnant slave girl but excepts the pregnancy. Hanafi law rules that the gift is valid and the exception is void, because a contract on the pregnancy alone (as a separate item) is invalid. The exception is treated as a void condition, and since a gift is not invalidated by void conditions, the entire slave girl including the pregnancy passes to the donee. The commentary from Al-Ikhtiyar elaborates that this ruling is one of three categories of exception of pregnancy: (1) contract valid, exception void (gift, marriage, khul', and reconciliation for intentional killing), (2) both void (e.g., sale of a pregnant animal with exception of its unborn), and (3) both valid (e.g., bequest). The key rationale is that the exception cannot operate where the contract itself would not be valid on the excepted item.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exclusion of part of a thing from a contract; here, excluding the pregnancy from a gift of a slave girl.
الشرط الفاسد (al-shart al-fasid): A condition that is void (fasid) because it is not permissible in the contract; here, the condition of excepting the pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which category does a bequest of a pregnant slave girl except her pregnancy fall into?
A) Contract valid, exception void
B) Both void
C) Both valid
D) Contract void, exception valid

Key Takeaway
The exception of pregnancy has three distinct categories in Hanafi law depending on the contract type: gift/marriage/khul'/sulh (contract valid, exception void), sale (both void), and bequest (both valid).

Suspension by Condition: Only for Pure Dropping of Rights and Oaths
States the maxim on ta'liq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on conditional suspension, identify which legal acts can be conditional, and apply the maxim to distinguish between isqat and tamlik.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, only pure drops of rights (isqat) and oaths can be suspended on a condition; transfers of ownership (tamlik) cannot.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle governing when a legal act may be made conditional upon a future event. The maxim 'التعليق بالشرط يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة ويحلف بها' succinctly captures the Hanafi position: only acts that constitute a pure dropping of a right, and oaths, can be validly suspended. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly structuring contracts, divorces, and other legal instruments in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define suspension (ta'liq) and identify the two categories
Understand that ta'liq means making a legal consequence dependent on a future condition. Acts are of two types: isqat mahd (pure dropping of rights) and tamlik (transfer of ownership). The maxim restricts ta'liq to the first category plus oaths.
Oaths are an exception to the isqat-only rule.
2
Identify examples of isqat mahd
Divorce (talaq), manumission (itq), renunciation of claims (ibra), and similar acts that extinguish a right without transferring it to another.
These are valid when conditional.
3
Identify examples of tamlik
Sales (bay'), gifts (hiba), leases (ijara), and any contract that transfers ownership or usufruct. These cannot be validly conditional.
If conditioned, the contract is void (batil).
4
Apply the maxim to a mixed case
Consider a statement: 'If you come, I gift you this book.' Since gift is tamlik, the condition invalidates the gift entirely. Contrast with: 'If you come, you are divorced.' Divorce is isqat, so the condition is valid and divorce occurs upon coming.
The consequence depends on the nature of the act.
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school permits ta'liq in all contracts, including sales and gifts, as long as the condition is valid. The Hanafi view is more restrictive, based on the idea that tamlik requires immediate certainty.
This difference has practical implications in drafting contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۰) اَلتَّعْلِيقُ بِالشَّرْطِ يَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِسْقَاطَاتِ الْمَحْضَةِ وَيُحْلَفُ بِهَا. ) ترجمہ: شرط پر معلق کرنا ان اسقاطات محضہ کے ساتھ خاص ہے جن کے ذریعہ قسم کھائی جاتی ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ان چیزوں کو شرط پر معلق کرنا درست ہے جو از قبیل اسقاط محضہ ہو اور جو چیز از قبیل تملیک ہو اس کو شرط پر معلق کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔
Suspension by condition is exclusive to pure drops (of rights) and oaths are taken by them. Translation: Suspending on a condition is specific to those pure drops of rights by which oaths are taken. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that it is valid to suspend on a condition those things that are of the category of pure drop (of rights), and it is not valid to suspend on a condition something that is of the category of ownership transfer.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage states a legal maxim regarding suspension (ta'liq) of legal acts on future conditions. The passage type is ruling statement. The principle 'التعليق بالشرط يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة ويحلف بها' establishes that in Hanafi law, suspending a legal act upon a condition (ta'liq bi al-shart) is only valid for acts that constitute a pure dropping of rights (isqat mahd) and for oaths. Transfer of ownership (tamlik) cannot be validly suspended on a condition; it must be unconditional to be effective. For example, a divorce (talaq) or manumission (itq) can be suspended on a condition because they are considered pure drops of rights (dropping the marital bond or dropping the right of ownership over a slave). In contrast, a gift or sale (which involve transfer of ownership) cannot be made conditional. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): Making a legal act conditional upon the occurrence of a future event.
الإسقاطات المحضة (al-isqatat al-mahda): Pure dropping of rights, such as divorce, manumission, or renunciation of a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following can be validly suspended on a condition according to this principle?
A) A gift (hiba).
B) A sale (bay').
C) A divorce (talaq).
D) A lease (ijara).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditional suspension is only valid for acts that purely drop a right and for oaths, never for transfers of ownership.

Exception of Pregnancy in a Gift is Void
Ruling with proof from al-Hidayah on void exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting a pregnant slave-girl with an exception of the pregnancy and explain why the exception is void but the gift remains valid.

Core Idea
An exception is void if the subject cannot be independently contracted; the gift itself stands.

This lesson examines a ruling from al-Hidayah on the validity of an exception (istithna) in a gift. The author establishes that an exception operates only where the contract can operate. Since a gift of a pregnancy alone is invalid, the exception is void. However, the gift itself remains valid because void conditions do not invalidate a gift in Hanafi fiqh. This illustrates broader rules about ta'liq bi al-shart and isqat mahd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is gifting a pregnant slave-girl with an exception of the pregnancy. The ruling: the gift is valid (sahih) and the exception is void (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the principle that 'the exception only operates in what the contract operates in.' Since a gift (hiba) of a pregnancy alone is invalid, the exception has no valid place.
This principle is cited from earlier in al-Hidayah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
A pregnancy is not a distinct legal entity at the time of contract; therefore, a gift of it alone is invalid. Consequently, excepting it is also invalid, becoming a void condition (shart fasid). Hiba remains valid because void conditions do not invalidate it.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage presents this as the established Hanafi position. Other schools may differ on the validity of such an exception, but the Hanafi school holds this ruling firmly.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
The practical consequence: the donee owns both the slave-girl and her pregnancy. The donor cannot retain the pregnancy.

Source Text

Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm bi dalil) from the Hanafi school regarding the exception of pregnancy in a gift. The author explains the principle that an exception (istithna) is only operative in a context where the contract itself is valid. Since a gift of a pregnancy alone is not valid (as it is not a distinct legal entity at the time of contract), an exception of the pregnancy from a gift of the slave-girl is void, and the gift remains valid. The principle is further generalized: suspension upon a condition (ta'liq bi al-shart) is specific to pure relinquishments (isqatat mahda) such as divorce and manumission, not to transfers of ownership (tamlik). The text then quotes from al-Ikhtiyar, dividing exceptions of pregnancy into three categories: (1) where the contract is valid and the exception void (gift, marriage, khul', and diyat reconciliation), (2) where both are void (e.g., sale), and (3) where both are valid (e.g., will). This detailed categorization shows the systematic nature of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): An exception made in a contract, e.g., excepting the pregnancy from a gift of a slave-girl.
تعليق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a legal consequence upon the occurrence of a future condition.
اسقاط محض (isqat mahd): A pure relinquishment of a right without any transfer of ownership, e.g., divorce, manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both the gift and the exception are valid.
B) The gift is void and the exception is valid.
C) The gift is valid but the exception is void.
D) Both the gift and the exception are void.

Key Takeaway
The exception in a gift is void if the excepted item cannot be independently gifted, but the gift itself remains valid.

Conditional Discharge of Debt is Invalid
Applies the principle that conditional discharge is invalid as it constitutes tamlik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh invalidates conditional discharge of debt and apply the distinction between tamlik (transfer of ownership) and isqat mahd (pure relinquishment) to determine when ta'liq (suspension) is valid.

Core Idea
Discharge of debt is tamlik, not isqat; tamlik cannot be suspended on a condition.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi ruling: a creditor cannot validly forgive a debt conditionally, such as 'if you pay half, the rest is forgiven.' This stems from the classification of ibra (discharge) as tamlik (transfer of ownership), which is legally incompatible with ta'liq (suspension). The ruling illustrates a broader Hanafi principle that only pure relinquishments (isqatat mahdah)—like talaq (divorce) and itq (manumission)—can be made conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional discharge of debt (ibra mu'allaq). The ruling is batil (void). The debt remains as it was.
Examples: 'If tomorrow comes, you are free from the debt' or 'If you pay half, the remaining half is forgiven.'
2
Understand the classification of ibra as tamlik
In Hanafi fiqh, ibra is not a mere renunciation (isqat) but a transfer of ownership (tamlik) because the creditor gives up his right to the debt, making the debtor the owner of that money.
Tamlik cannot be suspended on a condition.
3
Apply the principle that ta'liq is only for isqat mahd
Suspending a legal effect on a future condition (ta'liq bi al-shart) is valid only for pure relinquishments, like talaq and itq. Since ibra is tamlik, it falls outside this scope.
The principle: 'التعليق بالشرط يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة' (suspension on a condition is specific to pure relinquishments).
4
Distinguish between isqat that accepts ta'liq and those that do not
Some isqat (like talaq, itq) can be used for oaths and accept ta'liq. Others (like dismissing an agent, restraining a ma'dhun slave, or discharge of debt) do not accept ta'liq because they are not pure relinquishments.
This distinction helps identify when a condition is valid.
5
Conclude the practical outcome
Since the conditional discharge is void, the debt remains fully owed. The condition has no legal effect.
The original debt continues unaffected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ ثالث 141 صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی شخص سے کسی کی کچھ قرض پر لی، مثلاً ہزار درہم، پھر قرض خواہ نے مقروض سے کہا إِذَا جَاءَ غَدْ فَهِيَ لَكَ أوْ أَنتَ بَرِئَءٌ مِنْهَا أَوْ إِذَا أَدَّيْتَ إِلَى النِّصْفِ فَلَكَ النِّصْفُ أَوْ أَنْتَ بَرِيءٌ مِنَ النِّصْفِ الْبَاقِي (کہ جب کل کا دن آ جائے تو وہ ہزار آپ کے، یا ان سے آپ بری ہیں، یا اگر تم نے آدھا مجھے ادا کر دیا تو باقی آدھا تمہارا ہے، یا تم باقی آدھے سے بری ہو،) تو یہ باطل ہوگا کیونکہ ہزار سے بری کرنا اسی طرح پانچ سو سے بری کرنا یہ اس کی ملک میں دینا ہے، یعنی قرض خواہ مقروض کو ہزار کا یا آدھے کا مالک بنارہا ہے جو کہ تملیک ہے اور تملیک کو شرط پر معلق کرنا درست نہیں ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی ہزار یا نصف کی براءت کو قید کرنا یعنی آئندہ کل پر معلق کرنا درست نہ ہوگا۔ تعلیق بالشرط اسقاط محضہ کے ساتھ خاص ہے، جبکہ یہاں تملیک کا پہلو ہے، بری کرنا تملیک ہے اور تملیک شرط کے ساتھ معلق نہیں ہوتی۔ تعلیق اسقاط محضہ کے ساتھ خاص ہے، جیسے طلاق اور عتاق وغیرہ، اب یہ حکم کسی اور کی طرف متعدی نہیں ہوگا، یہ اُن میں سے ہے جن پر قسم کی جاتی ہے، اور ایک وہ اسقاط محضہ ہے جن پر قسم نہیں لی جاتی، یعنی جو تعلیق بالشرط کو قبول نہیں کرتے، جیسے عبد مأذون پر پابندی لگانا، وکیل کو معزول کرنا، قرض سے بری کرنا وغیرہ، ان پر قسم نہیں لی جاتی۔ فقال إذا جاء غد فهي لك أو أنت برىء منها وإذا أديت إلى النصف فأنت برىء من النصف الباقى فهذا كله باطل، لأن الإبراء تمليك و التعليق بالشرط يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة كالطلاق والعتاق فلا يتعداها. (الجوهرة النيرة: كتاب الهبة، ج ۳ ص ۳۳۲) ومن كان له على آخر ألف درهم فقال إذا جاء غد فهى لك، أو أنت برىء منها، أو قال: إذا أديت إلى النصف فلك النصف أو أنت برىء من النصف الباقي فهو باطل) فإذا كان باطلا يكون الألف عليه على حاله لأن الإبراء
Principles of Hidayah, third part, 141. The author of Hidayah states the mentioned principle that if someone owes something to another, e.g., a thousand dirhams, then the creditor said to the debtor: 'If tomorrow comes, it is yours, or you are free from it, or if you pay half to me, the half is yours, or you are free from the remaining half' – then this is all invalid, because discharging from a thousand or from five hundred is giving ownership to him, meaning the creditor makes the debtor owner of the thousand or the half, which is a transfer of ownership, and suspending a transfer of ownership upon a condition is not valid. Therefore, in the mentioned case also, tying the discharge of the thousand or half to a future condition is not valid. Suspension upon a condition is specific to pure relinquishments, whereas here there is an aspect of transfer of ownership; discharging is transfer of ownership, and transfer of ownership is not suspended upon a condition. Suspension is specific to pure relinquishments, like divorce and manumission, etc.; this ruling does not extend to others. It includes those upon which oaths are taken, and there are pure relinquishments upon which oaths are not taken, meaning those that do not accept suspension upon a condition, such as restraining a ma'dhun slave, dismissing an agent, discharging from debt, etc.; oaths are not taken upon them. He said: 'If tomorrow comes, it is yours, or you are free from it, and if you pay half, you are free from the remaining half – all of this is invalid, because discharge (ibra) is a transfer of ownership, and suspension upon a condition is specific to pure relinquishments like divorce and manumission, so it does not extend beyond them.' (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Hibah, vol. 3, p. 332) 'One who has a thousand dirhams owed to him and says: If tomorrow comes, it is yours, or you are free from it, or he said: If you pay half, you have half, or you are free from the remaining half – it is invalid.' If it is invalid, the thousand remains as it was, because al-ibra...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school explaining that a conditional discharge of debt (ibra mu'allaq) is invalid. The author applies the previously established principle: ta'liq bi al-shart (suspension on a condition) is only valid for pure relinquishments (isqatat mahda), not for transfers of ownership (tamlik). Discharge of debt (ibra) is considered tamlik because it involves giving up a right of ownership over the debt, thereby making the debtor the owner of that debt. Therefore, suspending it on a future condition, such as 'if tomorrow comes' or 'if you pay half,' is void, and the debt remains as it was. The text further distinguishes between two types of isqatat mahda: those that can be used for oaths (like divorce and manumission) and those that cannot (like restraining a ma'dhun slave or dismissing an agent). This distinction is important in determining the scope of ta'liq. The ruling is supported by a quote from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, emphasizing that the condition does not affect the debt.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra): Discharge of a debt, considered a transfer of ownership (tamlik) because the creditor gives up his right to the debt, making the debtor the owner of that money.
تعليق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a legal consequence on a future condition, valid only for pure relinquishments (isqatat mahda) like talaq and itq.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership, e.g., giving ownership of a debt to the debtor through discharge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A creditor says to the debtor: 'If you pay half of the debt, the remaining half is forgiven.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The forgiveness is valid if the condition is fulfilled.
B) The entire condition is void, and the full debt remains.
C) The forgiveness is valid only if stated as an oath.
D) Half the debt is forgiven automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional discharge of debt is void because ibra is tamlik, and tamlik cannot be suspended on a condition; the debt remains fully owed.

beginner
6 min
The Required Effect of a Contract Is Established Without Explicit Stipulation
Explains that the inherent legal effect of a contract applies even if not explicitly stated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that the inherent legal effect of a contract applies without explicit stipulation and apply it to the case of kitaba.

Core Idea
A contract's legal effect binds automatically—explicit words are not needed.

Contracts are not just empty words; they carry inherent consequences that the law recognises even if not spoken aloud. The Hanafi school, through the principle 'mujab al-aqd yathbut min ghayr al-tasrih', grounds this in rational necessity. This lesson unpacks that principle using the kitaba contract of manumission and the familiar analogy of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Locate the statement 'mujab al-aqd yathbut min ghayr al-tasrih' in the text. Understand that it declares the legal effect (muqtada) of a contract exists automatically without explicit verbal condition.
The principle is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Understand the proof (qiyas)
The proof is a qiyas jaliy: just as a sale contract transfers ownership without the parties explicitly stating 'ownership is transferred', the kitaba contract grants freedom without explicit declaration. Identify the asl (sale), far (kitaba), and illa (the contract inherently necessitates its effect).
The illa is that the contract is the cause (musabbib) and the effect (musabbab) follows necessarily.
3
Apply to the mukatab case
A mukatab (slave under kitaba) pays the full kitaba amount. Even if the master never said 'you are free', the slave becomes free because freedom is the inherent effect of the kitaba contract.
This is the direct application of the principle.
4
Distinguish from fasid contracts
The passage also references a fasid ijarah example to show that when a contract is invalid (fasid), its effect is not the stipulated wage but ajr al-mithl. This confirms that only valid contracts carry their intended effect automatically.
This contrast reinforces the principle's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب
(۱۳۰) مُوجَبُ الْعَقْدِ يَثْبُتُ مِنْ غَيْرِ التَّصْرِيحِ .
ترجمہ: عقد کا مقتضاء بغیر تصریح کے بھی ثابت ہوتا ہے۔
0
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو عقد کا مقتضی اور موجب ہوتا ہے اس پر صراحت نہ بھی کریں تب بھی وہ ثابت ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ عقد خود مقتضی ہے اور مقتضی جب ہوگا مقتضی بھی ہو گا، صراحت کرے یا نہ کرے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مکاتب جب بدل کتابت ادا کرے گا تو آزاد ہو جائے گا اگر چہ آقا نے اُسے یہ نہ کہا ہو کہ تو جب بھی بدل کتابت ادا کرے تو تو آزاد ہے، کیونکہ بدل کتابت کی ادائیگی کی بعد آزاد ہونا یہ بدل کتابت کے عقد کا مقتضی ہے اور جو عقد کا موجب ہوتا ہے وہ بغیر صراحت کے بھی ثابت ہوتا ہے، لہذا یہاں بھی مکاتب کی آزادی آقا کی صراحت کے بغیر بھی ثابت ہوگی۔ جیسے بیج میں صراحت نہ ہو تب بھی ملکیت ثابت ہو جاتی ہے، اس لیے کہ یہ بیع کا موجب ہے جو بغیر صراحت کے بھی ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔
(قال) أى فى الجامع الصغير: (ومن دفع إلى حائك غزلا لينسج بالنصف) فالإجارة فاسدة فلذلك قال: فله أجر مثله أى فللحائك أجر مثله، لأن هذا حكم الإجارة الفاسدة. قال والأصح عندى أن ما ذكره فى الكتاب أصح، لأن هذا في معنى قفيز الطحان على ما يجيء عن قريب. وقالوا فى شرح الجامع وكذلك إذا استأجر حمارا ورجلا يحمل طعاما بقفيز منه محمولا فالإجارة فاسدة ويجب أجر المثل البناية: كتاب الإجارات، باب الإجارة الفاسدة، ج ۱۰ ص ۲۹۷) الهداية: كتاب المكاتب، ج ۳ ص ۳۲۰
وَيُعْتَقُ إِذَا أَدَّى جَمِيعَ بَدَلِ الْكِتَابَةِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَقُلُ الْمَوْلَى إِذَا أَدَّيْتِهَا فَأَنْتَ
Kitab al-Mukatab (130) The required effect of a contract is established without explicit stipulation. Translation: The requirement of the contract is established even without explicit mention. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that what is required and necessitated by the contract is established even if one does not explicitly state it, because the contract itself necessitates it, and when the necessitator exists, the necessitated also exists, whether one states it explicitly or not. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, states that when a mukatab pays the entire kitaba amount, he becomes free even if the master did not tell him 'when you pay it you are free', because freedom after payment is the requirement of the kitaba contract, and what is required by the contract is established without explicit stipulation. Therefore here the mukatab's freedom is established without the master's explicit statement. Just as in a sale, even if not explicitly stated, ownership is established because it is the effect of the sale which is established without explicit mention. (He said, i.e., in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Whoever gives thread to a weaver to weave for half [the cloth], the ijarah is fasid.' Therefore he said: 'He has the ajr al-mithl', i.e., the weaver gets ajr al-mithl, because this is the ruling of fasid ijarah. He said: The soundest according to me is what he mentioned in the book, because this is in the meaning of qafiz al-tahhan as will come shortly. They said in Sharh al-Jami': Similarly, if one hires a donkey and a man to carry food for a qafiz of it, the ijarah is fasid and ajr al-mithl is wajib. Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Ijarat, Bab al-Ijarah al-Fasidah, vol. 10, p. 297) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Mukatab, vol. 3, p. 320. And he becomes free when he pays all of the kitaba amount, even if the master did not say 'When you pay it, you are free.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage introduces a key principle in Hanafi contract law, particularly in the context of kitaba (contract of manumission by payment). The principle states that the legal effect (muqtada/required result) of a contract is established automatically without needing explicit stipulation. The author illustrates this with the case of a mukatab (slave contracted to purchase freedom): upon paying the entire kitaba amount, he becomes free even if the master never explicitly stated that payment would lead to freedom. This is because freedom is the inherent effect of the kitaba contract. The same reasoning applies to sale (bay'): ownership transfers automatically upon contract completion even if not verbally stated. The passage also reiterates the earlier ijarah ruling as a contrast, showing that in a fasid ijarah, the effect is ajr al-mithl, not the agreed wage. The Arabic quote from al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Binayah reinforces the ijarah ruling. Thus, the principle is that contracts have inherent legal consequences that apply without additional verbal conditions.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
بدل کتابت (badal al-kitaba): The price or payment agreed upon in the kitaba contract for manumission.
موجب العقد (mujab al-aqd): The legal effect or consequence that a contract necessarily produces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave enters into a kitaba contract to pay 1000 dirhams for his freedom. He pays the full amount, but the master never said 'you are free'. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave remains a slave until the master explicitly declares him free.
B) The slave becomes free because freedom is the effect of the kitaba contract.
C) The contract is void because the slave paid without the master's statement.
D) The slave becomes free only if the master verbally confirms after payment.

Key Takeaway
The inherent legal effect of a valid contract is established automatically without the need for explicit verbal stipulation, as demonstrated by the automatic freedom of a mukatab upon full payment.

Ownership of a Thing Includes Its Necessities: Application to the Mukatab
States a legal maxim and its application to the mukatab's gifting capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that ownership of an object includes its necessities and apply it to determine what a mukatab may give as a gift.

Core Idea
The mukatab may give only trivial trade-related gifts because he owns his trade but not his overall property.

This lesson presents a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: ownership of a thing implies ownership of its accessories and necessities. The principle is illustrated through the case of the mukatab (a slave under kitabah). Understanding this maxim helps clarify limited ownership scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Components
Read the Arabic maxim: 'مَنْ مَلَكَ شَيْئًا يَمْلِكُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ ضَرُورَاتِهِ وَتَوَابِعِهِ' (Whoever owns something owns what is from its necessities and accessories). Identify the key terms: ضَرُورَات (necessities) and تَوَابِع (accessories).
This is a general rule of ownership in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the Context: The Mukatab's Partial Ownership
Recognize that a mukatab is a slave who has contracted kitabah, giving him partial ownership of his earnings while his master retains residual ownership. He is permitted to trade but not to give away property freely.
The mukatab's ownership is not absolute like a free person's.
3
Apply the Maxim: What Does the Mukatab Own?
The mukatab owns his trade activities (tijārah). Therefore, according to the maxim, he also owns the necessities of that trade. Minor gifts—such as small presents to clients—are considered necessities for maintaining trade relationships.
The maxim extends ownership to what is required for the proper functioning of the owned thing.
4
Derive the Ruling on Gifts
From the application: the mukatab may give minor gifts that are necessary for trade, but he cannot give substantial gifts or charity because those are not necessities of his trade and fall outside his ownership scope.
The ruling distinguishes between permissible minor trade gifts and prohibited substantial gifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ مَلَكَ شَيْئًا يَمْلِكُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ ضَرُورَاتِهِ وَتَوَابِعِهِ.
الهداية: كتاب المكاتب، باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله، ج ۳ ص ۳۲۵
Whoever owns something owns what is from its necessities and accessories. Al-Hidayah: Book of the Mukatab, Chapter on What the Mukatab is Allowed to Do, vol. 3 p. 325.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents another Hanafi principle: ownership of a thing includes ownership of its necessities and accessories. The author applies this to the mukatab. The mukatab does not fully own his property (since his master has residual ownership), so he cannot validly give gifts or charity from it. However, he is allowed to give minor, trivial gifts because these are considered necessities of trade (tijārah), and the mukatab is permitted to engage in trade. Since he owns the trade enterprise (as a partial owner through kitabah), he can do what is necessary for trade, including small gifts. The principle is that whoever owns something (e.g., trade) also owns its prerequisites (e.g., minor expenditures for goodwill). The text from al-Hidayah likely elaborates on these rulings, but the author here only cites the reference. The core idea is that the mukatab's ability to give is severely restricted, but his trade-related minor acts are an exception because they are necessary for his permitted trade.

Key Terms
ضَرُورَات (ḍarūrāt): Necessities or essential accessories of a thing; without which the thing cannot function properly.
تَوَابِع (tawābi‘): Accessories or incidental things that accompany the main owned item.
مُكَاتَب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a kitabah contract; he has partial ownership of his earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle in this passage, what type of gift is the mukatab allowed to give?
A) Any gift of any value.
B) A gift of a house or large property.
C) A minor gift that is necessary for his trade.
D) Charity to the poor in large amounts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that ownership of a thing includes its necessities means a mukatab may only give minor trade-related gifts, not substantial ones.
